<leme no="604">

<page sig="a1r">
<section type="Terms of Gardening">
<heading>An Explication of the Terms of Gard'ning, in
an Alphabetical Order.</heading>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="A"><alpha>A.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="ablaqueate(n)">TO <f type="i">Ablaqueates,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">or lay bare the Roots
of Trees. See Bare, and Trees and
Roots.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="acclivity(n)"><f type="i">Acclivity</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is the sloping of the side of a
Hill, or Bank, or Ridge, or any other
Ground not level, considered as Rising or
Ascending, which when considered as de&shy;
scending, is called <f type="i">Declivity.</f> See <f type="i">Declivi&shy;
ty.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr" lexeme="ados(n)"><f type="i">Ados,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a French Term signifying some&shy;
times a sloping <f type="i">Bank</f> raised against some
well exposed <f type="i">Wall,</f> to sow hasting or early
<f type="i">Pease</f> or <f type="i">Beans</f> in, or plant <f type="i">Artichokes,</f> or any
thing else we would have more forward
than ordinary, and sometimes Ridges or
Double Slopes, with Furrows <emend err="orDrains">or</emend> Drains be&shy;
tween them, to lay the Plants dry, in wet or
marshy, or over moist Grounds. See <f type="i">Banks,
Hillocke</f> and <f type="i">Slopes.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="agriot(n)"><f type="i">Agriots,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">in French, <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Griots</term>,</f> are a sort of
choice <f type="i">Cherries,</f> of the sharp sort, such as are
Four right <f type="i">Kentish Cherries.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="alberdge(n)"><f type="i">Alberdge,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a name given to <f type="i">Peaches,</f>
that are but of a small, or scarce midling
Size.</xpln><lemenote>"alberdge" not found in OED.</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="align(v)">To <f type="i">Aline,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is to range, level, or lay even
in, and to a strait and direct Line. Said of
Walls, Rows of Trees, and sides of Banks,
Allies, or Beds, which is performed with
Lines fastned to Spikes fixed in the Ground
or Wall, as is amply described in its proper
place. See to Range, to Level.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="aliner(n)"><f type="i">Aliners,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are such Rangers, or Men im&shy;
ployed in the abovesaid work of Ranging,
or Levelling Rows of <f type="i">Trees, Walls,</f> &amp;c. It
were well our English Gard'ners would
naturalize those two Words, not being
otherwise able to express their signification
without a Circumlocution, and having
with less necessity naturalized many other
forreign terms, without so much as alter&shy;
ing their Termination, which in these I
have made perfectly English.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="alley(n)"><f type="i">Allies,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="walk(n)">are such as we call Walks in any
Garden. See Walks, and their Use and
Proportion, see in the Body of the Book.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="alley(n)"><f type="i">Allies,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are said to be <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Bien Tirr&eacute;es, Bien Re&shy;
pass&eacute;es,</term></f> or <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Bien Retirr&eacute;es</term>,</f> that is, <f type="i">well plain'd,</f>
when they are laid smooth and firm and
tight again, with the beater or rouling
Stone after they have been scraped or tur&shy;
ned up with an Instrument to destroy the
Weeds.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="diagonal alley(n)"><f type="i">Diagonal Allies.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Diagonal.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="parallel alley(n)"><f type="i">Parallel Allies.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Parallel.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="amend(v)">To <f type="i">Amend,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is to <f type="i">Meliorate,</f> recruit, or
improve any Ground that is either exhau&shy;
sted by continual bearing, or that is natu&shy;
rally Barren, with <f type="i">Dung, Marl, Compost,
Fresh Mold,</f> or any other usual way of im&shy;
provement.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="amendment(n)"><f type="i">Amendment,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="mucking(n)" lexeme="dunging(n)">is <f type="i">Mucking, Dunging,</f> or any o&shy;
ther way recruiting or improving of Ground
as abovesaid.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="amputation(n)"><f type="i">Amputation,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is the sloping or cutting off,
of any considerable Branch or Limbs of a
Tree.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="annual plant(n)" lexeme="annual flower(n)"><f type="i">Annual Plants</f> or <f type="i">Flowers,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are such as con&shy;
tinue but a Year.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="ant(n)" lexeme="pismire(n)" lexeme="emmet(n)"><f type="i">Ants, Pismires,</f> or <f type="i">Emets,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are known
<f type="i">Insects.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="approach(v)" lexeme="graff(v)"><f type="i">Appro<emend err="l">a</emend>ch, to <f type="i">Graff</f> by <f type="i">Approach.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Graffing</f>
and <f type="i">Inoculation,</f> in the first part of this
Work.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="apple(n)" lexeme="pome(v)">To <f type="i">Apple</f> or <f type="i">Pome.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See to <f type="i">Pome.</f></xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="argot(n)" lexeme="spur(n)"><f type="i">Argots</f> or <f type="i">Spurs,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are the pointed ends
and extremities of dead Branches in any
Trees, which no neat Gard'ner will neg&shy;
lect to cut off. But it is particularly ne&shy;
cessary to do it in Nurseries for Trees graf&shy;
sed <f type="i">Scutcheon-wise.</f> See <f type="i">Spurs.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="arm(n)"><f type="i">Arms</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are the main Branches or Limbs of
a Tree.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="aromatic plant(n)"><f type="i">Aromatick</f> Plants,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are such as are Spicy,
and hot in Scent and Tast, whether sweet
or no.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="artichoke eye(n)" lexeme="eyelet(n)"><f type="i">Artichoke-Eyes</f> or <f type="i">Eyelets,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are the <f type="i">off-sets</f>
growing about the main <f type="i">Stool</f> or <f type="i">Heart</f>
of <f type="i">Artichoke Roots,</f> from which Spring the
<f type="i">Suckers</f> or <f type="i">Slips</f> by which they are propa&shy;
gated.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="artichoke sucker(n)"><f type="i">Artichokes Suckers</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="headed sucker(n)" lexeme="slip-sucker(n)" lexeme="eyelet(n)">are of two sorts, <f type="i">viz. Hea&shy;
ded Suckers,</f> that bear small Heads, and
shoot out of their stems round about their
main <f type="i">Heads,</f> but grow not so big, or <f type="i">Suckers</f>
which as is abovesaid, spring from the <f type="i">Off-&shy;
setts</f> of their <f type="i">Main Roots,</f> called by the <f type="i">French,
<term lang="fr">Orilletons</term>,</f> or <f type="i">Eyelets,</f> which are therefore
their <f type="i">Slips</f> or <f type="i">Slip-suckers.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="apothecary(n)"><f type="i">App</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Exposure</f> and <f type="i">Exposition.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="avenue(n)"><f type="i">Avenves,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="by-walk(n)" lexeme="counter-walk(n)">are certain Allies or Walks in
Gardens larger than ordinary, but more
properly leading to the front of the Houses,
which are commonly accompanied with
two Bye-Walks, co<emend err="mmm">mm</emend>only call'd Coun&shy;
ter-Walks, which are both Bordered with
great Trees, either <f type="i">Elms, Linden Trees,</f> or
<f type="i">Oaks,</f> and sometimes Standard Fruit-Trees.</xpln></wordentry>
</page>

<page sig="a1v">
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="aviary(n)"><f type="i">Aviary,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a convenient place in a Garden,
or House where Birds are kept, to Sing,
Breed, <f type="i">&amp;c.</f> </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="augusted(a)"><f type="i">Augusted,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="pompion(n)">is a Term used to signifie any
thing that is Sun burnt, and has endu&shy;
red the heat of the Summer, and is turned
ripe and yellow like Corn in <f type="i">August,</f> and
hard and firm withall. It is spoken of se&shy;
veral things, as of <f type="i">Branches</f> of <f type="i">Trees</f> that
are of a full Summers growth, of <f type="i">Melons,
Pumpions,</f> &amp;c. when they are grown yel&shy;
low, and hard, and will endure the Nail.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="arrest melons(v)" lexeme="arrest cucumbers(v)">To <f type="i">Arrest Melons</f> or <f type="i">Cucumbers,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is to
break off the top of their Vines, to check
and stop their Growth. See <f type="i">Melons.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="azarole(n)"><f type="i">Azerolls,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="garden-haw(n)">are <f type="i">Garden-Haws,</f> being twice
as big as the Wild ones, and much more Pulpy
and Pleasant, but not so fruitful;
and consequently, <f type="i">Azeroll-Trees,</f> <emend err="a">o</emend>r <f type="i">Garden-&shy;
Haw-Trees,</f> which are usually planted against
<f type="i">Walls.</f></xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="B"><alpha>B.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="backward fruit(n)" lexeme="backward legume(n)">B<f type="i">Acward</f> Fruits or <f type="i">Legumes,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are such as
ripen late in the year. See <f type="i">Latter.</f></xpln><lemenote>Headword phrases not found in OED.</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="long rye straw(n)" lexeme="straw(n)"><f type="i">Bands</f> of <f type="i">Long Rie Straw,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">steeped in wa&shy;
ter to make it lithe and pliant, and then
twisted, are made use of to tie up <f type="i">Lang Let&shy;
tuce,</f> or other plants to cabbage, or whiten.
Such Straw in <f type="i">French</f> is called <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Pleion</term>.</f> See
<f type="i">Straw.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="platband(n)"><f type="i">Plat-Bands.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Borders;</f> See likewise
under P.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="sloped bank(n)"><f type="i">Sloped Banks,</f> raised against Walls well
exposed, for the sowing or planting of Ha&shy;
sting or Early Peas, <f type="i">Beans, Artichokes,</f> &amp;c.</f></form>
<xpln lang="en">See <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Ados</term></f> and <f type="i">Slopes.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bank(v)">To <f type="i">Bank,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is to tight up the sides of a
Bed or Border, and make it firm with
the back of a Spade or other Instru&shy;
ment.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bare roots of trees(v)" lexeme="ablaqueate(n)">To <f type="i">Bare the <f type="i">Roots of Trees,</f> or to <f type="i">Abla&shy;
queate,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is to dig away the Earth about
them, and expose them sometime to the Air,
to refresh them, and make room for put&shy;
ting fresh Mold to them, or in order to
come at their Roots, to prune them, when
either defective, or too Rank or Luxuriant.
See <f type="i">Trees</f> and <f type="i">Roots.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bark(n)"><f type="i">Bark,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is the outward rind of a Tree.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bark(v)">To <f type="i">Bark</f></form> <xpln lang="en">a Tree, is to peal and strip it
of its Bark.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="basket of ozier(n)"><f type="i">Baskets</f> made of <f type="i">Ozier</f> wrought so loose,
that one may see through them,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="circumposition(n)">are used for
<f type="i">Circumposition,</f> that is to plant young Trees
in, to keep in reserve, to be ready to take
up again with Basket and all, to replant in
the places of such Trees as shall happen to
die, or otherwise miscarry, in the Ranks
where we had planted them, that so there
may be no Gaps, nor other Deformity in
any Rank of <emend err="Trets">Trees</emend>. The <f type="i">French</f> term them
<f type="i"><term lang="fr">Mannequine</term>.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bear(v)">To <f type="i">Bear</f></form> <xpln lang="en">easily or quickly, or easily or
quickly to take to, or to be brought to
bearing, is spoken of Trees that bear in
few years, or little time after they are plan&shy;
ted.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bear hardly(v)" lexeme="be hard to take(n)">To <f type="i">Bear</f> hardly, or to be hard to take,
or to be brought to bearing,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is spoken of
Trees that are many years or a long time
after planting, before they bear.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="beaten(a)"><f type="i">Beaten,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">spoken of tilled Ground when
it is so beaten with great and violent Rains,
that 'tis grown as firm as if it had been ne&shy;
ver tilled at all.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="beater(n)">A <f type="i">Beater</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is an Instrument wherewith
<f type="i">Gravel Walks, Pall-malls,</f> &amp;c. are smoothed
and made firm.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bed(n)"><f type="i">Beds</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are plots of dressed Ground, which
in digging, are wrought into such a form
by the Gard'ner, as is most conveni&shy;
ent to the temper and situation of the Earth
in that place, and to the nature of the
Plants to be sown or planted in it. They
are of two sorts, <f type="i">Cold</f> and <f type="i">Hot.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="cold bed(n)"><f type="i">Cold Beds</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="natural earth(n)">are made either of <f type="i">Natural
Earth,</f> or mixed and improved <f type="i">Mold,</f> and
are in moist Grounds raised higher than the
Paths, to keep them moderately dry, and
in rising and dry Grounds, laid lower than
the Paths, that they may on the contrary
retain moisture so much the better, and pro&shy;
fit so much the more by the Rain that
falls.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="hotbed(n)"><f type="i">Hot Beds,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="long new dung(n)">are <f type="i">Beds</f> composed of <f type="i">Long
New Dung,</f> well packt together, to such a
height and breadth as is prescribed in the
Body of the Book, and then covered over
to a certain thickness, with a well tempered
Mold, in order to the planting or sowing
such plants in them, as are capable of be&shy;
ing by Art, forced to grow, and arrive to
maturity even in the midst of <f type="i">Winter,</f> or at
least a considerable while before their na&shy;
tural Season.

How these <f type="i">Beds</f> are differently made
for <f type="i">Mushrooms</f> and how for other Plants.
See in the work it self.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="deaf bed(n)"><f type="i">Deaf Beds</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are such <f type="i">Hot Beds</f> as are made
hollow in the Ground, by taking away
the natural Earth to such a certain depth,
and filling the place with Dung, and then
covering it with <f type="i">Mold,</f> till it rise just even
with the Surface of the Ground. They are
used for <f type="i">Mushrooms.</f></xpln><lemenote>"deaf bed" not found in OED.</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="kernel bed(n)"><f type="i">Kernel Beds</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are <f type="i">Nursery Beds,</f> wherein the
Seed or Kernels of Kernel Fruit are sown in
order to raise Stocks to Graff upon.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bell(n)"><f type="i">Bells,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are large <f type="i">Glasses</f> made in the form
of <f type="i">Bells,</f> to clap over tender Plants or such
as are to be forced, to keep them from the
Cold, and to communicate the <f type="i">Sun</f>'s heat to
</page>

<page sig="a2r">
them sometimes with the more advantage,
when it is not strong enough without them. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr" lexeme="belles de nuit(n)"><f type="i">Belles de Nuit,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">or fair ones of the night,
are a sort of <f type="i">Flowers.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bigarreau(n)"><f type="i">Bigarros,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are a sort of <f type="i">French Heart Cher&shy;
ries,</f> so called because they be partly Red,
and partly White.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bigarade(n)"><f type="i">Bigarrades,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are a sort of Sour <f type="i">Oranges</f>
deep coloured, and in a manner crumpled,
and with a very uneven Rind, used chiefly
in Sauces, and to medicinal purposes. So
called from their odd and uncouth shape.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bigarade(n)"><f type="i">Bigarrades,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are a sort of mothey colour&shy;
ed <f type="i">Pears,</f> called also the vilonous <f type="i">Pears</f> of
<f type="i">Anjou.</f></xpln><lemenote>Sense of "bigarade" as 'pear' not found in OED.</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="biting(n)"><f type="i">Biting</f></f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a taste in <f type="i">Fruits</f> and Plants,
well known to any taster.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr" lexeme="le blanc(n)"><f type="i">Le Blanc,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">i. e. the <f type="i">Whites,</f> is a sort of
disease in <f type="i">Melons</f> and <f type="i">Cucumbers,</f> termed only
in <f type="i">English Unhealthiness:</f> See <f type="i">Unhealthi&shy;
ness.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="blanch(n)">To <f type="i">Blanch:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See to <f type="i">Whiten.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="blast(n)" lexeme="blasting(n)"><f type="i">Blast</f></form> <xpln lang="en">and <f type="i">Blasting,</f> are said of any
young <f type="i">Buds</f> or <f type="i">Flowers,</f> or Leaves of Plants,
that being nipt by the <f type="i">East</f> or <f type="i">North East</f>
Winds sometimes reigning in the <f type="i">Spring,</f>
wither and shrivel, and fall off, to give
place to new ones. The <f type="i">Authour's</f> counsel
is to ease the Plants, by taking them spee&shy;
dily off.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bleed(v)">To <f type="i">Bleed,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">or <f type="i">Weep</f> is said of <f type="i">Vines</f> whose
sap runs out to waste by indiscreet cutting
or any other accident: See weep.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="blighing(n)"><f type="i">Blighing</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is said of <f type="i">Flowers</f> or <f type="i">Blossoms,</f>
that shed or fall without knitting for <f type="i">Fruit,</f>
by the effect of the same <f type="i">Winds.</f></xpln><lemenote>Headword not found in OED: "blighting"?</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bloom(v)">To <f type="i">Bloom</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is said of any Plant that begins
to <f type="i">Flower.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="blossom(n)"><f type="i">Blossomes</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are well known to be the <f type="i">Flowers</f>
of <f type="i">Fruit-Trees.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="blossom(n)"><f type="i">Blossoms</f> that shed without knitting for
<f type="i">Fruit,</f></f></form> <xpln lang="en">are said in <f type="i">French <term lang="fr">Couler</term>,</f> i. e. to slip,
or slink like an <f type="i">Abortive Birth.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="blow(v)">To <f type="i">Blow,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is said of <f type="i">Flower Buds</f> when
they open and spread.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="border(n)"><f type="i">Borders</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are <f type="i">Flat Banks</f> raised against <f type="i">Walls</f>
commonly garnish'd with <f type="i">Sweet Scented
Herbs,</f> as <f type="i">Time, Sage, Lavender,</f> &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bordure(n)"><f type="i">Bordures</f> or <f type="i">Borders,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a term likewise used
for <f type="i">Herbs</f> commonly planted in <f type="i">Borders.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="counter-borders(n)" lexeme="plat-band(n)"><f type="i">Cou<emend err="">n</emend>ter-borders,</f> or <f type="i">Plat Bands,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are such
<f type="i">Borders</f> as are made on the sides of <f type="i">Walks</f> or
<f type="i">Squares</f> that front the <f type="i">Borders</f> by <f type="i">Walls.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="border an alley(v)">To <f type="i">Border</f> an <f type="i">Alley,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is to <f type="i">Line</f> or <f type="i">Edge</f> it
with <f type="i">Borders</f> that may separate it from the
<f type="i">Squares,</f> and <f type="i">Beds,</f> and other works contain&shy;
ed in them, between which they Traverse.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="botanist(n)">A <f type="i">Botannist,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is one that is a profinement
in the general knowledge of Plants.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bottle of hay(n)" lexeme="truss of hay(n)">A <f type="i">Bottle</f> or <f type="i">Truss</f> of <f type="i">Hay,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a known Term.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bough(n)"><f type="i">Bough:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Branches.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="box(n)"><f type="i">Boxes:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Cases.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="branch(n)" lexeme="bough(n)">A <f type="i">Branch</f> or <f type="i">Bough</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a part of a <f type="i">Tree,</f>
that shooting from the <f type="i">Trunk,</f> helps to form
the Head. <f type="i">Branches</f> are of several sorts.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="augusted branch(n)"><f type="i">An <f type="i">Augusted Branch</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a <f type="i">Branch</f> of a
<f type="i">Summers</f> growth, that is just hard'ned, and
has done growing.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bearing(n)" lexeme="fruit-branch(n)">A <f type="i">Bearing,</f> or <f type="i">Fruit-Branch</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a young
shoot that is grown of a midling length and
thickness from the pruning of the foregoing
year.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="collateral branch(n)"><f type="i">Collateral Branches,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are such <f type="i">Branches</f> as
grow and spread on the same side of a <f type="i">Wall-&shy;
Tree.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="mother branch(n)">A <f type="i">Mother Branch,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a <f type="i">Branch</f> that after
its last <f type="i">Pruning,</f> has shot forth other new
<f type="i">Branches;</f> And thus we say, that in <f type="i">Pruning</f>
no young <f type="i">Branches</f> are to be left upon the
<f type="i">Mother Branches,</f> but such as contribute to
the Beauty of the figure of the <f type="i">Tree.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="main branch(n)" lexeme="whole branch(n)"><f type="i">Main,</f> or whole <f type="i">Branches</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="arm(n)" lexeme="limb(n)">are called
<f type="i">Arms,</f> and <f type="i">Limbs.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="scrubbed branch(n)" lexeme="shrubby branch(n)"><f type="i"><term lang="fr">Branches Chifonnes.</term> Skrubbed</f> or <f type="i">Shrubbie
Branches</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are such as are very small and
very short or <f type="i">Skrubbed,</f> whether they be of
but of one or more years growth, and be&shy;
cause they do but stuff a <f type="i">Tree</f> with a confu&shy;
sed quantity of <emend err="unless">useless</emend> Leaves, must there&shy;
fore be taken clean away.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="water branch(n)" lexeme="water shoot(n)" lexeme="water bough(n)"><f type="i">Water Branches,</f> or <f type="i">Water shoots,</f> or <f type="i">Water
Boughs,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are such <f type="i">Boughs</f> that on Standards,
being shaded and dript <emend err="u!">u</emend>pon, remain smooth
and naked without <f type="i">Buds</f> which are as I sup&shy;
pose those which our <f type="i">Authour</f> calls <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Jarrets</term></f> or
<f type="i">Hams:</f> See <f type="i">Hams.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="wood branch(n)">A <f type="i">Wood Branch,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a <f type="i">Branch</f> that spring&shy;
ing out of the last years <f type="i">Pruning</f> in a regular
and natural order, is reasonably thick.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="half-wood branch(n)">A half <f type="i">Wood Branch,</f> or <f type="i">Branch</f> of half
<f type="i">Wood,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a <f type="i">Branch</f> that being too small for
a <f type="i">Wood Branch,</f> and two thick for a <f type="i">Fruit
Branch,</f> must be shortned to the length of
two or three Inches, to make it produce
other new ones that may be better either
for <f type="i">Wood,</f> or <f type="i">Fruit,</f> and at the same time
contribute to the Beauty of the figure, and
to spend off and divert some of the over
Luxuriant vigour of the <f type="i">Tree.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="false-wood branch(n)"><f type="i"><emend err="Branchss">Branches</emend></f> of <f type="i">False Wood,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are such as
shoot from any other part of the <f type="i">Tree</f> than
that which was <f type="i">Pruned,</f> last year; or else
su<emend err="n">c</emend>h as though they spring from the last
<f type="i">Pruning,</f> are thick and gouty in those places
where they should be slender.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="main running branch(n)"><f type="i">The main <f type="i">Running Branches</f> of <f type="i">Melons</f> or
<f type="i">Cucumbers</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="vine(n)">are called <f type="i">Vines.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="break up(n)">To break up,</form> <xpln lang="en">Is said properly of plow&shy;
ing or digging up Ground that never was

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="browse(v)"><f type="i">To <f type="i">Brouse</f> or <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Brout</term>,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">in <f type="i">French, <term lang="fr">Brouter</term>,</f> is

</page>

<page sig="a2v">
ches,</f> when they are too long in proportion

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bruised fruit(n)"><f type="i">Bruised Fruit,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="squatted(a)">that is, bruised in falling

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bud(n)">A <f type="i">Bud</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is the <f type="i">Head</f> of a <f type="i">Young shoot</f> that
begins to peep out.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="young bud(n)" lexeme="eye(n)">A <f type="i">Young Bud</f> or <f type="i">Eye,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a <f type="i">Bud</f> as 'twere
in its first Seed and principles, when it just
only appears in the <f type="i">Bark</f> of the <f type="i">Tree,</f> before
it swells to peep or shoot forth.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bud(v)">To <f type="i">Bud,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="inoculation(n)">is to <f type="i">Graff</f> by <f type="i">Inoculation,</f> or
set a young <f type="i">Bud</f> of one <f type="i">Tree</f> into another;
an operation to be performed about <f type="i">Mid-&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="well budded tree(a)" lexeme="well-set tree(n)" lexeme="trees(n)"><f type="i">Well <emend err="Buddod">Budded</emend></f> or <f type="i">well set Trees;</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is said of

<f type="i">Fruit Buds,</f> and the contrary of those that

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bucket(n)" lexeme="tub(n)">A <f type="i">Bucket</f> or <f type="i">Tub</f></form> <xpln lang="en">used by <f type="i">Gard'ners</f> some&shy;
times, to sow some choice particular Seeds
in; they are made sometimes square or ob&shy;
long, but most commonly round, and about
the bigness of a Barrel. The <f type="i">French</f> call
them <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Baquets</term>.</f> See <f type="i">Tubs.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bull(n)" lexeme="bulbous root(n)" lexeme="roots(n)"><f type="i">Bulls</f> or <f type="i">Bulbous Roots,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are all such <f type="i">Roots</f> as are
Roundish and Coat upon Coat like <f type="i">Onions,</f> as
those of <f type="i">Garlick, Tulips,</f> &amp;c. and are most&shy;
ly propagated by <f type="i">Off-sets.</f> And accordingly
in <f type="i">French</f> they are called by the general
name of <f type="i">Onions.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="mother bub(n)"><f type="i">Mother Bubs,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are those which produce <f type="i">Off-&shy;
sets.</f></xpln><lemenote>"bub" not found in OED in this sense.</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bunch(n)">A <f type="i">Bunch</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a common Term, as a <f type="i">Bunch</f>
of <f type="i">Radishes, Turneps,</f> &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bunch(n)"><f type="i">Bunch</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is also said of <f type="i">Grapes</f> or any <f type="i">Fruit</f>
that produces several <f type="i">Fruit</f> upon one stalk;
as also of knots of <f type="i">Wood,</f> &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="burly-tree(n)"><f type="i">Burly-Trees</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are said to be or grow <f type="i">Burly,</f>

it is <f type="i">Graffed</f> upon, which is <emend err="a sign">asign</emend> the stock

<f type="i">French</f> term is <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Burlet</term>.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bubo(n)"><f type="i">Bubo,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is said of some <f type="i">Fruit Shrubs,</f> as a
<f type="i">Curran-Bush,</f> a <f type="i">Goose-berry-Bush,</f> &amp;c. Also
the tops of <f type="i">Dwarf-Trees</f> are said to be <f type="i">Bushie,</f>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="button(n)">A <f type="i">Button</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a round and turgid swelling

<f type="i">Fruit</f> in any <f type="i">Tree;</f> in Kernal <f type="i">Fruit</f> every

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="cabbage(v)" lexeme="pome(v)">To <f type="i">Cabbage</f> or <f type="i">Pome,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is to curl or fold
up into a round firm head like a <f type="i">Cabbage,</f>
or an <f type="i">Apple.</f>

Thus not only <f type="i">Cabbage</f> but <f type="i">Lettuce</f> is said
to <f type="i">Cabbage</f> or <f type="i">Pome,</f> and <f type="i">Artichokes</f> are said to

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="calebas(n)"><f type="i">Calebas</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="plumping(n)">is a term used for <f type="i">Plums</f> that in
the Month of <f type="i">May</f> instead of Plumping or
preserving their <f type="i">Green</f> grow broad, lank,
and Whitish, and at last fall off without

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="canker(n)">A <f type="i">Canker</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="scurf(n)" lexeme="scabbiness(n)">is a sort of <f type="i">Scurf, Scabbiness,</f> or
dry <f type="i">Rot</f> in <f type="i">Trees,</f> which breeds both in the
Bark and in the <f type="i">Wood,</f> and most infests, the
little <f type="i">Muscat</f> and <f type="i">Robine,</f> and <f type="i">Bergamot Pear-&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="capron(n)"><f type="i">Caprons</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are <f type="i">Straw-berry</f> Plants that have

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="carpet walk(n)">A <f type="i">Carpet Walk</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a <f type="i">Green Walk</f> of <f type="i">Grass,

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr" lexeme="courtilliere(n)"><f type="i">Courtilliere,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="palmer-worm(n)">is a sort of insect, or Palmer

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="dung(n)"><f type="i">Dung,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a known Term, and is long
and new, or short and old. Long and new
fresh Dung is Litter that has served Horses
or Mules but one or two Nights at most,
and has all its straw entire in it, and has
not yet fermented, and much less rotted,
old and short Dung, is Dung that has fer&shy;
mented and lost its heat, and whose Straw
is rotted, and formed into a kind of Mold
with the Dung.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="dwarf tree(n)"><f type="i">Dwarf Trees</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="low standard(n)">are Low Standards, or

as to have but low Trunks, and moderate&shy;
ly spreading Branches and Tops.</xpln><lemenote>"low standard" not found in OED (but see "standard," n., 20).</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="embroidery(n)">E<f type="i">Mbroidery,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a term used in <f type="i">Flower Gar&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="earth(n)"><f type="i">Earth,</f> in <f type="i">Gard<emend err="">'</emend>ning,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="white clay(n)" lexeme="gravelled(a)" lexeme="fallow ground(n)">is taken for the
Soil or Ground in which <f type="i">Trees Legumes,</f> or
Edible and useful Plants or their Seeds are
to be sown or planted, and is of several sorts,
as for example:

It is call'd <f type="i">Sower, Bitter,</f> and <f type="i">Stinking,</f>
when in smelling to it, or taking the water
in which it has soaked, we perceive it <f type="i">Sowr
bitter</f> or <f type="i">stinking.</f>

It is called White <f type="i">Clay,</f> when it is of a
White stiff, and slimy substance, and is
fat, heavy, gross and Cold, and cuts like
Butter, and is very apt to chop with the
<f type="i">Summers</f> heat; and some call it dead Earth
because of its unfruitfulness.

It is stiled good when we can make any
thing grow in we have a mind to; And
bad when neither <f type="i">Trees,</f> Plants nor Seeds
thrive in it.

It is called hot and burning, when it is
so light and dry that upon the least heat,
all the Plants in it dry away and wither.

It is called <f type="i">Gravelled</f> when 'tis mixed
with much sand and many little stones tem<emend err="">&shy;</emend>
pered with a little light Red <f type="i">Clay.</f>

It is called <f type="i">Tough,</f> heavy, and by some
stubborn, and because of its unfruitfulness
Chast, and in <f type="i">England</f> Red, Loamy stiff
<f type="i">Clay,</f> when it cuts smooth and stiff, and is
very hard to Till or dress, because the great
rains beat it all into a marsh like mortar,
and the heat on the other side chops it,
and makes it hard as a stone.

It is called strong, free, or rank Earth,
when without being stiff and Clayie, it is
like the bottom or mould under the turf,
of good medow Ground, and in handling,
sticks to the fingers like a paste, and receives
any shape or impression from them, whe&shy;
ther long, round, <f type="i">&amp;c.</f>

It is termed Cold, moist, and backward
when upon the advance of the <f type="i">Spring</f> it is
long before it conceives heat enough to put
forth its productions, and brings forth eve&shy;
ry thing later than other places.

It is called forward, or hasty, when
<f type="i">Fruits</f> ripen in it betimes; as at St. <f type="i">Ger&shy;
mains Paris,</f> St. <f type="i">Maur,</f> &amp;c. and back&shy;
ward when it has a contrary effect.

It is called loose, light and mellow, when
either by art or nature it is brought to a
midling consistence, that is, loose and light
like sand, and yet partaking of the heart
and substance of good mould, easily obeying
the spade, rake and other instruments, and
penetrable to Seeds and the shoots and Fi&shy;
brous Roots of Plants and <f type="i">Trees.</f>

It is called new or fresh when it never
served yet to the production of any Plant,
such as is found two or three foot or more
in depth beyond the surface or upper crust
of the Ground.

It is called made or transported Earth
when 'tis brought into the <f type="i">Garden</f> from
some other place.

It is called Fallow Ground when 'tis laid
to rest a year or two or more, without being
planted or sown with any thing. See hol&shy;
low.

It is called over wrought or exhausted
Ground, when it has been a long time con&shy;
tinually tilled, sown and Planted without
intermission, and without any recruit or
amendment.

Lastly it is called light and sandy when
without having any body of true Earth, its
parts do not stick together, no not with
the Rain it self, but are so loose, that no
plants can take hold enough to fix any
Root there.</xpln></wordentry>
</page>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="mould(n)"><f type="i">Mould</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is transported Earth, and is either
Natural or Artificial. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="natural mould(n)"><f type="i">Natural <f type="i">Mould,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is pure choice well tem&shy;
pered Earth, taken from the bottom or un&shy;
der pasture of a Meadow, or other place
where the Earth is naturally rank and mel&shy;
low, or made out of the castings of Ditches
or Ponds, well dryed, sunned, and dres&shy;
sed, <f type="i">&amp;c.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="artificial mould(n)"><f type="i">Artificial <f type="i">Mould,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is Earth composed of
rotten Dung, natural Mold, rotted Leaves
of Trees, and other proper fanting and en&shy;
riching Materials. See <f type="i">Compost.</f></xpln><lemenote>Possibly "fanting" should be emended to "planting."</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="earth up(v)">To <f type="i">Earth</f> up,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is to bank or hillock up the
Earth about <f type="i">Cellery, Endive, Long Lettuce,
Chards,</f> &amp;c. almost to their Tops, to whiten
them.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="edging(n)"><f type="i">Edgings,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are the edges of Borders or Beds

<f type="i">Herbs.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="espalier(n)"><f type="i">Espaliers,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are <f type="i">Wall-trees,</f> or any Trellissed,
or pallisado'd Tree.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="estiloer(n)"><f type="i">Estiloers.</f></form> <xpln lang="en"></xpln><lemenote>Unidentified.</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="evergreen(n)"><f type="i">Ever Greens</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are such Plants, whose Leaves

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="exhausted(a)"><f type="i">Exhau<emend err="t">s</emend>ted.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See Earth.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="exotic(n)"><f type="i">Exoticks,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are Foreign Plants brought

turally grow in our Climates.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="exposition(n)" lexeme="exposure(n)" lexeme="aspect(n)"><f type="i">Exposition, Exposure</f> and <f type="i">Aspect,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">signifie
the same thing, and denotes the posture or
situation any Wall, or plant is in, in order
to receive the Benefit of the Rays, and

Northern, Southern, Eastern, Western.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="good exposition(n)">The <f type="i">Good Expositions,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are those of the
<f type="i">East</f> and <f type="i">South,</f> whereof the <f type="i">South</f> is the best.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bad exposition(n)">The <f type="i">Bad Exposition</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is that of the <f type="i">North.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="middling exposition(n)" lexeme="indifferent exposition(n)">The <f type="i">Midling</f> or <f type="i">Indifferent Exposition,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is

the Body of the Work.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="eye(n)"><f type="i">Eye,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">a young Bud just appearing in the

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="faggots(n)">F<f type="i">Aggots,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is the brushy or small part in

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="fallow earth(n)" lexeme="ground(n)"><f type="i">Fallow Earth</f> or <f type="i">Ground,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is <f type="i">Ground</f> laid to
rest, and only tilled, mucked, and amended,
without sowing or planting any thing in
for a certain time till it be recruited. See
<f type="i">Earth.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="false flower(n)" lexeme="false blossom(n)"><f type="i">False <f type="i">Flowers</f> or <f type="i">Blossoms,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are those Flow&shy;
ers or Blossoms of <f type="i">Cucumbers, Melons,</f> &amp;c.
that do not knit, or set for Fruit, but fall off
without producing any thing.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="false wood(n)"><f type="i">False Wood.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Branches.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="fang(n)"><f type="i">Fangs,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a term signifying the Claws
of <f type="i">Ranunculus,</f> and such like Roots, with
which they take hold in the Ground. See
<f type="i">Claws.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="fan(v)" lexeme="screen(v)" lexeme="corn(n)" lexeme="seeds(n)">To <f type="i">Fan</f> or <f type="i">Skreen</f> Corn or Seeds,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a
well known Term.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr" lexeme="fane(n)"><f type="i">Fane,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a <f type="i">French</f> Term, the top or leafy
part, properly of such Plants whose Leafs
are only or mostly in use, as of <f type="i">Radishes,
Turneps,</f> &amp;c. being that part of them, as
the word imports, that is, subject to fade
and wither. It is called in English, some&shy;
times <f type="i">Foliage.</f> See <f type="i">Foliage, Tops, Leaves.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="fathom(n)">A <f type="i">Fathom</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a measure of six Foot, called
in <f type="i">French,</f> a <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Toise</term>,</f> and is a Term much
used by this Author. See <f type="i">Toise.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="feed(v)"><f type="i">Feed</f></form> <xpln lang="en">as to feed Vines with Blood, and
other nourishing and refreshing mixtures.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="fibre(n)"><f type="i">Fibres</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are small long Roots like Hairs,
that spring out of the larger Roots of
Trees</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="fill(v)" lexeme="knit(v)">To <f type="i">Fill</f> or <f type="i">Knit,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="plump(v)">is said of Fruit when it

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="rouse(n)" lexeme="arrouse(n)"><f type="i">Fine,</f> as <f type="i">Roses,</f> or <f type="i">Arroses</f> fine,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are gentle

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="flea(n)"><f type="i">Fleas</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are a sort of black Vermine, that

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="flavour(n)"><f type="i">Flavour,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a grateful tast, mixed with a

when we say they have a fine Flavour.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="fill(v)" lexeme="knit(v)">To <f type="i">Fill,</f> or <f type="i">Knit,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is said of Fruit when

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="florist(n)">A <f type="i">Florist,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a Gard'ner that cultivates

stands and delights in the same.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="foliage(n)"><f type="i">F<emend err="e">o</emend>liage</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is the leafy part of a Tree or

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="forest-tree(n)"><f type="i">F<emend err="e">o</emend>rest-Trees</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are such Trees as bear no

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="fork(v)"><f type="i">Fork</f> and to <f type="i">Fork</f> the Earth, <f type="i">&amp;c.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="forward fruit(n)" lexeme="forward plant(n)" lexeme="forward legume(n)"><f type="i">Forward Fruits, Plants</f> or <f type="i">Legumes,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="fothered ground(n)"><f type="i">Fotherd Grounds,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is ground upon which

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="free stock(n)">A <f type="i">Free-stock,</f> as <f type="i">Free-stock</f> upon <f type="i">Free-stock,</f></form>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="frost-bitten(a)"><f type="i">Frost-bitten,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is said of Blossoms, Buds,

<page sig="a4v">
are killed, or otherwise spoild with the Frost. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="fruit-loft(n)" lexeme="garner(n)" lexeme="room(n)" lexeme="storehouse(n)" lexeme="magazine(n)">A <f type="i">Fruit-loft,</f> Garner, Room, Store-house,

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="kernel-fruit(n)"><f type="i">Kernel-Fruit,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is Fruit that comes of Ker&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="stone-fruit(n)"><f type="i">Stone Fruit,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is Fruit that comes of, and

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="furniture(n)"><f type="i">Furnitures,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are all hot and spicy Herbs,

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="fold in(v)">To fold in,</form> <xpln lang="en">is said of Leaves of decayed

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="foot of a tree(n)">The <f type="i">Foot</f> of a Tree</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="neck of a tree(n)">is that swelling part
of a Tree between the Body or Trunk, and

the Earth. It is sometimes called in <f type="i">French,</f>
the Neck of a Tree.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="level slope(n)">A <f type="i">Level</f> slope,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="talus(n)">is a slope that rises or falls

cernible from a plain, or true level. It is
called in <f type="i">French <term lang="fr">Micote</term>,</f> and when made so

<f type="i">Nursery Beds,</f> and prick them into other Beds
at more distance.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="prop(v)">To <f type="i">Prop,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is to prop up any Plants with
Perches, forked Sticks, or Poles, such as
<f type="i">Hops, Vines, Peas, French-Beans,</f> &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="prime(v)">To <f type="i">Prime,</f> and its several ways.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See in
the Treatise of Pruning.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="pulp(n)"><f type="i">Pulp,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is the inward Substance or fleshy
part of any Fruit, of which there are seve&shy;
ral sorts, as Buttred and melting Pulp, is
that which is melting and sweet in the
Mouth, like Butter, such as is that of the
<f type="i">Butter-pear, Bergamots,</f> &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="short pulp(n)"><f type="i">Short <f type="i">Pulp,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is that which breaks short
in eating, such as is that of <f type="i">Pears,</f> that are
firm without being hard, and that crackle
between the Teeth in eating.

It is called tough harsh and hard in cer&shy;
tain <f type="i">Pear,</f> that have nothing of fine or de&shy;
licate, as in <f type="i">Catillac's, Double-flowers,</f> &amp;c.

It is called Mealy, when it eats dry and
mealy, as in over ripe <f type="i">Dean-pears, Cadet-pears,</f>
&amp;c.

It is called Doughy, when it is fattish
and disagreeably soft like Dough, as in white
<f type="i">Butter-pears, Lansacs</f> that grow in the shade.

It is called Tender, in certain Pears, that
though they be neither melting nor short,
yet are tender and excellent, without being
soft, fatty, or otherwise distastful, as in un&shy;
known <f type="i">Chaineaus Vine Pears.</f>

Lastly, some <f type="i">Pears</f> have sower taste, as
the St. <f type="i">Germain Pears,</f> and some sharp and
biting as the <f type="i">Crasauns.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="punaise(n)" lexeme="bug(n)">A <f type="i">Punaise</f> or <f type="i">Bug</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a sort of a <f type="i">Tyke</f> that
preys upon Plants, as the stinking Bugs of
the same Name do Human Bodies.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="pure(a)"><f type="i">Pure.</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="plain(a)">See <f type="i">Plain.</f></xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="Q"><alpha>Q.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="quince stock(n)">Q<f type="i">Uince Stocks,</f> that are smooth, strait,
vigorous, and fit to graff upon,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">the
Author calls <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Coignassiers</term>,</f> and those that are
rough, knotty and skrubbed and unfit, he
calls <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Coigniers</term>.</f> But he believes them not
Male and Female, according to the vulgar
Fancy. Of these the <f type="i">Portugal</f> are best.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="refresh(v)">To <f type="i">Refresh,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="ablaqueation(n)">is said in two Senses, <f type="i">viz</f>
First, Trees are refreshed by Ablaqueation
i. e. by laying their Roots bare, and re&shy;
trenching their decayed and superfluous
Roots, and recruiting them with good fresh
Earth, or well tempered Mould, or by tur&shy;
ning up side down, and well dressing and
stirring the old Earth.

<f type="i">Secondly, To Refresh,</f> is likewise to wa&shy;
ter Trees or Plants, as also to feed them,
and diet them with Water diluted with
Milk, or well tinged with Dung, or other
rich ingredients, or with Bloud, or other
fatning and nourishing things, when they
are Sick.</xpln></wordentry>

a <f type="i">Vine</f> or other <f type="i">Fruit-Tree</f> that is not
yet hardned or grown Woody.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="terrace(n)">A <f type="i">Terrass,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is an artificial bank or mount
of Earth, commonly supported with a
fronting or facing of stone, and raised like
a kind of <f type="i">Bulwark</f> for the ornament of a
<f type="i">Garden.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="thin(v)">To thin,</form> <xpln lang="en">is to pick off <f type="i">Fruit,</f> pull up
<f type="i">Herbs</f> and Roots, or cut away <f type="i">Branches</f>
when they grow too thick, that the rest
may thrive the better.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="ticket(v)">To <f type="i">Ticket,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="ticket(n)">is to fasten <f type="i">Tickets</f> or notes
about <f type="i">Fruit-Trees</f> <emend err="containg">containing</emend> their names and
order to distinguish them.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="till(v)">To <f type="i">Till,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is to dig, delve, plow, and o&shy;
therwise dress or prepare the Ground for
planting or sowing.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="toise(n)" lexeme="fathom(n)"><f type="i">Toise</f> or <f type="i">Fathom</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a measure of six foot
see <f type="i">Fathom.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="cubical toise(n)">A <f type="i">Cubical Toise</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is the 216 foot every
way of any thing measur'd by the <f type="i">Toise</f> or
<f type="i">Fathom.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="ton(n)"><f type="i">Ton</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a sort of <f type="i">Worm</f> or Maggot that
gnaws <f type="i">Straw-berry</f> Roots.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="trail(n)">A <f type="i">Trail</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a Trelliss, or Lattice frame
made for the support of Wall and palisa&shy;
ded <f type="i">Trees.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="transplant(v)">To <f type="i">Transplant,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is to take up any thing
out of the <f type="i">Bed</f> or place where it was sown,
when it is grown to a fit bigness, and to
plant or set in another place where 'tis to
remain, or to be improved to a greater per&shy;
fection.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="tread(v)">To <f type="i">Tread,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is used in more sences than one,
as to tread down Earth about <f type="i">Trees,</f> &amp;c.
To make it settle firmly, <f type="i">&amp;c.</f> or to tread,
as <emend err="the the">the</emend> tops of <f type="i">Carrots, Parsnips,</f> &amp;c. are
troden down to keep the sap from moun&shy;
ting, that it may nourish the Roots the bet&shy;
ter, <f type="i">&amp;c.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="trench(v)"><f type="i">To Trench,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is to dig the Ground up, and
</page>

<page sig="b3v">
to make <f type="i">Trenches,</f> furrows and holes to
plant <f type="i">Trees, Artichokes,</f> &amp;c. in. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="trench(n)"><f type="i">Trenches,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are Furrows with Holes fitted
for Trees, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="trellis(v)">To <f type="i">Trelliss,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is to pallisade, nail up and
fasten Trees upon Walls, or Pole-Hedges,
and on wooden Trails or Trelisses.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="truss of hay(n)">A <f type="i">Truss</f> of Hay,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a Term well known</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="truss up(v)">To Truss</f> up,</form> <xpln lang="en">is to raise up a Branch of a
<f type="i">Wall-Tree</f> that hangs down, and tack it up
fast, that the Fruit may not break it, or
disfigure the Tree by Swagging it down
with its weight.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="turn up earth(v)" lexeme="loosen earth(v)">To <f type="i">Turn</f> up or loosen the Earth:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See stir,
and <f type="i">Till.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="turf(n)">A <f type="i">Turf,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is either a Turf of Grass with
its Earth, or so much Earth that hangs firm
about the Root of a Tree, or Plant or Tuft
of any Plant that grows in Tufts, when
they are pulled up.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="tuft(n)"><f type="i">Tuft,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a knot of Roots or Boughs, as
'twere united together in one round Body
or Cluster; so we say, a Tufted or Bushy
Tree, a Tuft of <f type="i">Strawberries,</f> &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="tyger-babbs(n)"><f type="i">Tyger-Babbs,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are a sort of pestilent insects,
infesting <f type="i">Wall-Pears,</f> and <f type="i">Pear-Trees.</f></xpln><lemenote>"tyger-babbs" not found in OED.</lemenote></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="V"><alpha>V.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="vein(n)">V<f type="i">Ein,</f> as a Vein of Earth</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is said Com&shy;
paratively of some parts of a Gar&shy;
den that produce better or worse than other
parts of it, whence we say, here is a good,
and there is a Bad Vein of Earth.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="vegetable(n)"><f type="i">Vegetables,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are all sorts of living Plants,
Trees, or Herbs that grow.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="vegetation(n)"><f type="i">Vegetation,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is the springing or growing

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="verdure(n)"><f type="i">Verdures,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a Term denoting all Plants

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="vermin(n)"><f type="i">Vermine</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are all misch<emend err="t">i</emend>evous Creatures that

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="vigorous(a)"><f type="i">Vigorous.</f></form> <xpln lang="en"></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="vineyard(n)"><f type="i">Vine-yard,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a piece of Tilled Ground

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="vine-dresser(n)"><f type="i">Vine Dressers,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are those Husbandmen

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="vine of melon(n)"><f type="i">Vines</f> of <f type="i">Melons,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are their main running

like Vine Branches.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="vinous(a)"><f type="i">Vinous,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is said of a Winy tast and smell

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="unbind(v)">To <f type="i">Unbind,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is to take off the Bands of

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="uncase(v)">To <f type="i">Uncase,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is to take any Plant out of

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="under pasture(n)"><f type="i">Under Pasture,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is Mold taken from under

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="unhealthy(a)"><f type="i">Unhealthy,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is said of <f type="i">Melons</f> or <f type="i">Cucum&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="unpot(v)">To <f type="i">Unpot,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is to take Plants out of Pots.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="wound(v)">To <f type="i">Wound,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="wound(n)">is so to cut Trees in Pruning

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="wrap(v)">To <f type="i">Wrap,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">as to wrap up Plants, or ten&shy;

<page sig="t2r">
<section type="Kitchen Garden">
<heading>CHAP. I.</heading>

<f type="i">What things should be planted in any</f> Kitchen-Garden <f type="i">of a reasonable extent,
to render it compleatly furnished.</f>

ALL the World is agreed, that there are few days in the whole Year in which
we can be well without the assistance of the <f type="i">Kitchen-Gardens,</f> whether it be in
the fair and fruitful Season, whilst they are still growing on the Earth that first pro&shy;
duced them, when we have no more to do but to go and gather them there; or in
<f type="i">Winter</f> when we must fetch them out of the Store-rooms, where we had timely laid them
up for Security, before the arrival of the bitter cold, which not only makes the Earth
unfruitful for a time, but destroys too a great part of those Vegetables which are so un&shy;
happy as to be within its reach; and therefore it follows, that every day of the Year,
we must be furnisht with what we need of this Nature, out of our own Gardens or
Stores, or elsewhere, either by the liberality of our Friends, or what is most common
from the Markets.

That you may therefore have at one view, the knowledge of what composes this a&shy;
greeable assistance, that may be drawn out of the <f type="i">Kitchen-Garden.</f> I shall here present
you with a kind of <f type="i">Alphabetical Inventory</f> of all the things that such a Garden should,
and may furnish us with, throughout the whole course of the year.

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="A"><alpha>A.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="cresson al&eacute;nois(n)">A<f type="i">Lenois Cresses.</f></form> <xpln lang="en"><f type="i">Vid.</f> Cresses.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="pt"><f type="i">Alfange,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">a sort of Roman <f type="i">Lettuce</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<f type="i">Sharp Trefoil.</f></xpln><lemenote>"Sharp trefoil" not found in OED.</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="anise(n)"><f type="i">Anis.</f></form> <xpln lang="en"></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="artichoke(n)"><f type="i">Artichokes,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="coston(n)" lexeme="slip-sucker(n)"><f type="i">Costons, or Slip-suckers.</f></xpln><lemenote>"coston" and "slip-sucker" not found in OED.</lemenote></wordentry>
