<section type="Seeds">
<heading>CHAP. II.</heading>

<f type="i">Containing a Description of the Seeds, and other things which contribute to
the production and Multiplication of every sort of Plant, or Legume.</i>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="A"><alpha><f type="i">A</f></alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="cresson al&eacute;nois(n)"><f type="i">Alenois Cresses:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Cresses.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="pt"><f type="i">Alfange:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Lettuce.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="alleluia(n)" lexeme="wood-sorrel(n)" lexeme="French sorrel(n)"><f type="i">ALLELUIA,</f> or <f type="i">Wood,</f> or <f type="i">French Sorrel,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="trefoil(n)">is a sort of <f type="i">Trefoil,</f> that is multiplied
only by Runners or slips, which sprout from the foot of it, as do <f type="i">Violets</f> and <f type="i">Dai&shy;
ses,</f> &amp;c. It bears a White Flower, but no seed.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="anise(n)"><f type="i">Anis,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">Is propagated only by seed, which is pretty small, and of a yellowish Green,
and is of a longish Oval Figure, Striped: Which Oval is Bunched on one side. In a
word, it is altogether like <f type="i">Fennel-seed.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="artichoke(n)"><f type="i">Artichoaks,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="eye(n)" lexeme="offset(n)" lexeme="kernel(n)" lexeme="sucker(n)" lexeme="slipping(n)">are commonly multiplied only by their <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Oeillitons</term></f> or little <f type="i">Eyes,</f> or Off-Sets
which are a sort of <f type="i">Kernals,</f> which grow about the heart of the foot of their plants,
<emend err="r">t</emend>hat is, in that part that separates the Root from the Eye or bud, out of which the
stemm grows that produces the <f type="i">Artichockes:</f> These little <f type="i">Eyes</f> or off-sets begin commonly
to breed at the very end of <f type="i">Autumn,</f> or in <f type="i">Winter,</f> when it is mild, and shoot forth their
Leaves in the <f type="i">Spring,</f> that is at the end of <f type="i">March,</f> and in the month of <f type="i">April,</f> at which
time, we grope about the foot of the <f type="i">Artichoke,</f> and separate or slip off these Suckers or
off-sets, in French called little Eyes, and that is called <f type="i">Slipping,</f> or <f type="i">dis-Eyeing.</f>

These off-sets or suckers to be good, should be White about the heel, and have some
little roots; those that are black about the heel, are old, and produce but very little <f type="i">Ar&shy;
tichokes</f> in the spring, whereas the others stay till <f type="i">August, September,</f> or <f type="i">October,</f> before
they bring theirs to perfection, according to the intention of the Gard'ner.

Sometimes <f type="i">Artichokes</f> are multiplied by the seed, which grows in the <f type="i">Artichoke</f> bottoms,
when they are suffered to grow old, to flower, and to open, and lastly to dry, about
<f type="i">Midsummer.</f>
</page>

<page sig="t3v">
When we tie them up in <f type="i">Autumn,</f> we wrap and cover them up to their whole length,
with straw or old dung, and so Whiten the <f type="i">Cottony</f> sides of their leaves, to make <f type="i">Arti&shy;
choke</f> Chards of.</xpln><lemenote>"dis-Eyeing" antedates earliest OED citation (1719).</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="asparagus(n)"><f type="i">Asparagus,</f> or <f type="i">Sparagras</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are propagated only by seeds which is black, a little oval,
round on one side, and very flat on the other, about the bigness of a great pin's head,
and grows in a shell, or round Cod, which is <f type="i">Red,</f> and about the bigness of an ordi&shy;
nary <f type="i">Pea;</f> there are four or six seeds in each shell, and those shells grow in <f type="i">Autumn,</f>
upon the head of those <f type="i">Asparagus</f> plants that are a little fairer and stronger than the
rest. Sometimes those shells are sown whole, but the best way is to break them, and
beat the seeds out of them. The time of sowing them is about the end of <f type="i">March.</f></xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="B"><alpha>B</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="balm(n)">B<f type="i">Alm,</f> in <f type="i">French, <term lang="fr">Melisse</term>,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is multiplied only by Runners and Cuttings.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="basil(n)" lexeme="basilic(n)"><f type="i">Basil,</f> or <f type="i">Basilick,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">as well the <f type="i">Great,</f> as the <f type="i">Small</f> sort is multiplied by seed, which is
of a blackish cinnamon colour, and very Small and a little oval, and is propagated no
other way but that.</xpln><lemenote>"Basilick" appears to be an anglicized French form but is not found in the OED as a spelling in English.</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bay(n)"><f type="i">The common <f type="i">Bay,</f> or <f type="i">Bays,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is propagated by seeds which are Black, or else by Lay&shy;
ers.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bean(n)"><f type="i">Beans,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="marsh bean(n)" lexeme="haricot(n)" lexeme="kidney bean(n)" lexeme="faverole(n)">as the <f type="i">Marsh</f> or <f type="i">Common Beans,</f> which are pretty thick and long, of an oval
figure, round at one end, and flat at the other, with a black list or Crease pretty thick
and broad, of a sullied White colour, having a smoother skin than the <f type="i">Haricauts,</f> or <f type="i">Kid&shy;
ney Beans,</f> which are likewise long and oval, but narrower, lesser, and thinner than the
other, having a black list in the middle of one of the sides of the oval, which is round
on one side, and a little bending Inward on the other. The <f type="i">Feverolles,</f> or <f type="i">Venetian
Beans,</f> differ only from these last, in that they are a little less, and are some of them
<f type="i">White,</f> some <f type="i">Red,</f> and some mottled with several Colours; there is one sort of them
that is very small. Every body knows, they all grow in Cods.</xpln><lemenote>"marsh bean" and "Venetian bean" not found in OED.</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="beet-rave(n)" lexeme="beet radish(n)"><f type="i">Beet-Raves,</f> or <f type="i">Beet-Radishes,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="red beet(n)">that is, <f type="i">Red-Beets</f> to produce Roots for Sallets, are mul&shy;
tiplied only by Seeds, which are about the bigness of middling <f type="i">Peas,</f> and round, but all
gravelly in their roundness; they are yellowish, and so like those of the <f type="i">White Beet,</f> that
they are hardly to be distinguish'd one from the other, so that People are often mista&shy;
ken, thinking they have sown <f type="i">Red Ones</f> for Roots, and see nothing come up but <f type="i">White
Beets;</f> they are planted apart when designed to run to Seed.</xpln><lemenote>Antedates earliest citation of "beet-rave" in OED (1719); "beet radish" not found in OED.</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="white beet(n)" lexeme="chard(n)"><f type="i">White Beets,</f> called <term lang="fr"><f type="i">Porr&ecirc;e</f></term> or <term lang="fr"><f type="i">Poir&eacute;e,</f></term> for <f type="i">Chards</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="red beet(n)">are also propogated only by Seed,
which is like that of the <f type="i">Red Beets,</f> only 'tis of a little duller colour: They are replanted
to produce <f type="i">Chards.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="good lady(n)"><f type="i"><term lang="fr">Bonne-Dame</term>,</f> or <f type="i">Good Lady,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is multiplied only by Seed, which is extreamly flat, and
thin, and is round and reddish.</xpln><lemenote>"good lady" unidentified.</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="borage(n)"><f type="i">Borage</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is propagated only by Seed, which is black, and of a long bunchy Oval Fi&shy;
gure, and having commonly a little white end towards the base or bottom, which is
quite separated from the rest, the length is all Engraven as 'twere with black streaks from
one end to the other.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bugloss(n)"><f type="i">Bugloss</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is likewise only multiplied by Seed, which is so like that of <f type="i">Borage,</f> that they
cannot be known asunder.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="buck's horn(n)"><f type="i">Buckshorn Sallet</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is multiplied only by Seed, which is one of the least we have; it is
besides that, longish, and of a very dark Cinnamon colour, and grows in a <f type="i">Husk</f> like
a Rats Tail.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="burnet(n)"><f type="i">Burnet</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is propagated only by Seed, which is pretty big, and a little Oval, with four
sides, and is all over engraven as 'twere in the spaces between those four sides.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="C"><alpha><f type="i">C.</f></alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="cabbage(n)">C<f type="i">Abbages,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="colewort(n)" lexeme="cauliflower(n)">called in <f type="i">French, <term lang="fr">Choux</term>,</f> and comprehending both <f type="i">Cabbage, Coleworts,</f> and
<f type="i">Colyflowers</f> of all kinds, of what Nature soever they be, are multiplied only by Seed,
which is about the bigness of an ordinary Pin, or of Birding Powder, and is reddish, in&shy;
clining to a brown Cinnamon colour.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="capucine caper(n)"><f type="i">Capucin Capers.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Nasturces.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="capron(n)"><f type="i">Caprons.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Strawberries.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Spanish cardoon(n)"><f type="i">Spanish Cardons</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are propagated only by Seed, which is longish, oval, and about the
bigness of a fair <f type="i">Wheat Corn;</f> it is of a greenish, or <f type="i">Olive</f> colour, mark'd with black
streaks from one end to the other, and is Sown from the middle of <f type="i">April</f> to the end.</xpln></wordentry>
</page>

<page sig="t4r">
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="carrot(n)"><f type="i">Carrots<f type="i"></form> <xpln lang="en">are multiplied only by Seeds, which are small and oval, the sides of which are
wrought with little streaks, or longish points very small; and one side of the flat part of
the Seed is a little fuller, and more raised than the other, and both of them are marked
long-ways with streaks; they are of the colour of a dead Leaf. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="celery(n)"><f type="i">Cellery</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is multiplied only by Seed, which is very small, yellowish, and of a longish
oval Figure, and a little bunched.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="shalot(n)"><f type="i">Chalots:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Eschalots.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="i">Chards</f> of <f type="i">Artichokes:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Artichokes.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="chard(n)"><f type="i">Chards</f> of <f type="i">Beets:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Beets.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="chervil(n)"><f type="i">Chervil</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is multiplied only by Seed, which is black, very small, and pretty longish;
striped long-ways; it grows upon the Plants that were Sown in the <f type="i">Autumn</f> before, and
Knits and Ripens in the Month of <f type="i">June.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="i">Musked Chervil</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is multiplied likewise only by Seed, which is longish, black, and pretty
big.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="chervil(n)" lexeme="skirret(n)"><f type="i">Chervi</f> or <f type="i">Skirrets</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is multiplied only by Seed, which is oval, longish, and pretty small
and narrow, streaked from one end to the other, and of the colour of a grayish white
dead Leaf, and flat at one end.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="ciboule(n)" lexeme="onion(n)"><f type="i">Ciboules,</f> or small <f type="i">Onions,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="leek(n)">are propagated only by Seed, of the bigness of a corn of or&shy;
dinary <f type="i">Gun-powder,</f> a little flat on one side, and half round on the other, and yet a little
long and oval, and white on the inside; so like to which are the Seeds of both the
<f type="i">Red</f> and <f type="i">White Onion,</f> and of <f type="i">Leeks,</f> that it is very hard to distinguish them one from
the other: <f type="i">Ciboules</f> are Sown in all Seasons.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="citrul(n)" lexeme="pompion(n)" lexeme="pumpkin(n)"><f type="i">Citrulls, Pumpions,</f> or <f type="i">Pumkins,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are propagated only by Seeds, which are of a flat oval
Figure, and pretty large and whitish, and are as 'twere neatly edged about the sides,
excepting only at the bottom, where they stuck to the <f type="i">Citrull</f> or <f type="i">Pumpion,</f> in whose Belly
they were formed.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="chive(n)"><f type="i">Cives,</f> called <f type="i">English Cives,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are multiplied only by little Off-sets that grow round about
their <f type="i">Tufts,</f> which grow very big in time, from which a part of those Off-sets are taken
to Replant.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="cauliflower(n)"><f type="i">Colyflowers:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Cabbages.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="colewort(n)"><f type="i">Coleworts:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Cabbages.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="cresson al&eacute;nois(n)"><f type="i">Cresses,</f> called <f type="i"><emend err="Al&egrave;nois">Al&eacute;nois</emend> Cresses,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are multiplied only by Seed, which is of a longish oval
figure, small, and of an <f type="i">Orange</f> yellow colour.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="cucumber(n)"><f type="i">Cucumbers,</f> or <f type="i">Cowcumbers,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are propagated only by Seed, which is oval, a little pointed
at both ends, but a little less at the lower end or bottom than at the other, out of which
springs its Bud or Sprout; it is of a midling thickness, of a whitish colour, and is gathered
out of the Bellies of those <f type="i">Cucumbers</f> that are grown yellow with ripeness.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="currant-bush(n)">The <f type="i">Curran-Bushes,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="gooseberry-bush(n)">whose Fruit grows in Bunches, both the Red, and the White,
called <f type="i">Dutch Currans;</f> as also <f type="i">Goosberry-Bushes,</f> called in <f type="i">French, <term lang="fr">Groseilles</term>,</f> as well as <f type="i">Cur&shy;
rans,</f> and named <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Picquans</term>,</f> or <f type="i">Prickly <term lang="fr">Groseilles</term>,</f> are multiplied as well by slips that are a
little Rooted, that Sprout out of the foot of their Stocks every year in the <f type="i">Spring,</f> as by
simple Cuttings; we also Replant their Stocks of two or three years old.</xpln><lemenote>"Dutch currant" not found in OED.</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="dragons(n)"><f type="i">Dragons,</f> or <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Estragon</term>,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">a <f type="i">Sallet:</f> See <f type="i">Estragon.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="endive(n)">W<f type="i">Hite Endive,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="succory(n)">called in <f type="i">French, <term lang="fr">Chicor&eacute;e</term>,</f> i. e. <f type="i">Succory,</f> is multiplied only by Seed,

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="garlic(n)">G<f type="i">Arlick</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="clove(n)" lexeme="stalk(n)" lexeme="shoot(n)">is produced by a kind of Kernels, or Off-sets, which grow in great numbers
about its Foot, <f type="i">and make all together a kind of Bulb like an Onion,</f> which Kernels
are called the <f type="i">Cloves</f> of the <f type="i">Garlick;</f> every <f type="i">Clove</f> being concave or hollow on the inside,
and convex, or bending outwards on the out-side, having at its lower end, a flat base or
bottom, by which it is fastned to the body of the Foot or Stalk, out of which the
Roots spring; and having on the top a pointed end, out of which springs its Bud, or
Shoot, when it is planted in the Earth in the Months of <f type="i">March</f> or <f type="i">April,</f> in order to its
bringing forth.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="laurel(n)"><f type="i">Lawrel,</f> or <f type="i">Laurel:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Bays.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="leek(n)"><f type="i">Leeks</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="ciboule(n)">are multiplied only by Seed, which is altogether like that of <f type="i">Ciboules;</f> they are
Replanted in the Month of <f type="i">May,</f> very deep in the Earth, to make their Stalks and
Plants thick and white; and they are Sown in <f type="i">March</f> as soon as the Frost will permit;
their Seed grows in a kind of thick white Purse, which is round, and grows upon the
top of a good long Stalk, and it keeps a pretty long time in that Purse or Hood before
it falls.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="lettuce(n)"><f type="i">Lettuces,</f> of what sort so ever they be,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="winter lettuce(n)" lexeme="lettuce of Auberviliers(n)" lexeme="shell lettuce(n)">are multiplied only by Seed, which is of a
longish oval figure, streaked long-ways, sharp pointed at the ends, and very small; some
are black, as those of <f type="i">Aubervilliers,</f> but the most of them are white: when they are Sown
in the <f type="i">Spring</f> they run to Seed in the Month of <f type="i">July</f> after; but the <f type="i">Winter Lettuces,</f> called
otherwise <f type="i">Shell Lettuces,</f> after having past the Winter in the place where they were Re-&shy;
planted in <f type="i">October</f> run up to Seed in the Month of <f type="i">July</f> following.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="M"><alpha><f type="i">M.</f></alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"lexeme="Macedonian parsley(n)">M<f type="i">Acedonian Parsly:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Parsly.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="m&acirc;che(n)"><f type="i">M&acirc;ches,</f> or <f type="i">Masches,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are multiplied only by Seed, which is very small, and of
an <f type="i">Orange</f> colour.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="mallow(n)" lexeme="marchmallow(n)"><f type="i">Mallows,</f> or <f type="i">Marsh-Mallows,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are propagated only by Seeds, which are like one another
in shape, but yet are different as well in colour as in bigness; for the Seed of the <f type="i">Mallows</f>
is much bigger than that of the <f type="i">Marsh-Mallows,</f> and that of this latter is of a deeper brown
than that of the plain <f type="i">Mallows;</f> they are both Triangular, and streaked all over.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="marjoram(n)"><f type="i">Marjoram</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is propagated only by Seed, which is very little, and shaped almost like a
<f type="i">Limon,</f> more pointed on one side than on the other; it is speckled in some places with
little white specks, and is as 'twere streaked with white all over; it is of a pretty light
Cinnamon colour.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="melon(n)"><f type="i">Melons,</f> or <f type="i">Musk-Melons,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are multiplied by a Seed, which is like that of a <f type="i">Cucumber,</f>
excepting in colour, which in Melons is of a pale red, and is not so broad as that of
the others; they are taken out of the Bellies of ripe <f type="i">Musk-Melons.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="mint(n)" lexeme="spearmint(n)"><f type="i">Mint,</f> or <f type="i">Spare-Mint,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">called in <f type="i">French, Balm,</f> is multiplied only by Runners that are
like so many Arms that spring out of its <f type="i">Tuft,</f> and take Root; it likewise is propagated
by Cuttings, but bears no Seed.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="muscat(n)"><f type="i">Muscat:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Vines.</f></xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</page>

<page sig="v1r">
<wordgroup1 type="a" object="N"><alpha><f type="i">N.</f></alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="nasturces(n)">N<f type="i">Asturces,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="capucine caper(n)">commonly called <f type="i">Capucin Capers,</f> are multiplied only by Seed, which is a
kind of <f type="i">Pea</f> or <f type="i">Haricot,</f> or <f type="i">French-Bean,</f> which climbs and gets up upon Branches or
Poles which are near it; the Leaf of it is pretty large, and the flower, of an <f type="i">Orange</f> colour;
the figure of the Seed is a little Pyramidal, divided by Ribs, having all its superficies engra&shy;
ven, and wrought all over, being of a grey colour, inclining to a light Cinnamon:
They are Sown in hot Beds about the end of <f type="i">March,</f> or the beginning of <f type="i">April,</f> and af&shy;
terwards they are Replanted by some Wall well exposed. The Seed easily falls as soon
as ever 'tis Ripe, as doth that of <f type="i">Borage,</f> and the <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Belles de Nuit</term>,</f> or <f type="i">Night Fair Ones;</f> and
therefore they must be carefully gathered.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="O"><alpha><f type="i">O.</f></alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="onion(n)"><f type="i">ONions,</f> as well the <f type="i">White,</f> as the <f type="i">Red,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are multiplied only by Seed, which as I have
already said, is like that of <f type="i">Ciboules.</f></xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="P"><alpha>P.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="parsley(n)">P<f type="i">Arsly,</f> as well the <f type="i">Common,</f> as the <f type="i">Curled sort,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is multiplied only by Seed, which is
little and very small, and of a greenish grey colour, and a little bending inward
on one side, and all over streaked with little rising streaks from one end to the other.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Macedonian parsley(n)"><f type="i">Macedonian Parsly</f> or <f type="i">Alisanders</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is also propagated only by Seed, which is pretty big and
oval, and a little more full and swelling on one side than on the other, which bends a lit&shy;
tle inward, streaked throughout its whole length; and is also streaked a cross on the edges
between the sides.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="passe-pierre(n)"><f type="i">Passe-pierre:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Pierce-Pierre.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="parsnip(n)" lexeme="passe-pierre(n)"><f type="i">Parsnips</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are multiplied only by Seed, which is flat, and of a round figure, a little oval,
and as if it were hemmed or edged, streaked throughout its length, and is of the colour
of a brownish Straw.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="patience(n)"><f type="i">Patience:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Dock.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr"><f type="i">Passe-Musquee:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Muscats,</f> and <f type="i">Vines.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="pea(n)" lexeme="pease(n)"><f type="i">Peas,</f> or <f type="i">Pease,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are multiplied only by Seed; there are <f type="i">great Ones, little Ones, white Ones</f>
or <f type="i">yellow</f> Ones, and <f type="i">green</f> Ones. All the world knows they grow in Cods, and are almost
round, and sometimes half flat.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="passe-pierre(n)"><f type="i">Perce-Pierre</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="stone parsley(n)" lexeme="pierce-stone(n)">vulgarly called <f type="i">Passe-Pierre,</f> i. e. <f type="i">Pass,</f> or <f type="i">Pierce Stone,</f> being a kind of
<f type="i">Stone-Parsly,</f> is multiplied only by Seed, which is more long than round, pretty big, of
a greenish grey colour, striped on the Back and Belly, and resembling a Lute in shape.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="pimpernel(n)"><f type="i">Pimpernell:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Burnet.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="pompion(n)" lexeme="pumpkin(n)"><f type="i">Pompions,</f> or <f type="i">Pumpions,</f> or <f type="i">Pumkins:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Citrulls.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="potiron(n)" lexeme="citrul(n)" lexeme="pompion(n)"><f type="i">Potirons,</f> a sort of <f type="i">Flat Citrulls,</f> or <f type="i">Pumpions,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are multiplied only by Seed, which is
altogether like that of the <f type="i">Common Citrull,</f> or <f type="i">Pumpion,</f> and grows in the same manner.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="purslane(n)"><f type="i">Purslain,</f> as well of the <f type="i">Green,</f> as <f type="i">Red,</f> or <f type="i">Golden sort,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is multiplied only by Seed, which
is black, and extraordinary small, and of a half flat roundish figure. To have a good
Crop of this Seed, the <f type="i">Purslain</f> Plants must be Replanted at the end of <f type="i">May,</f> at a full
Foot distance one from the other: The Seed grows in little Husks or Shells, each of
which contain a great many, and when we are to gather it, we cut off all the heads of
the Stalks, and lay them to dry a little in the Sun, and then we beat the Seed out, and
Fan, or Screen it.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="R"><alpha><f type="i">R.</f></alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="radish(n)">R<f type="i">Adishes</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are multiplied by Seed, which is round, pretty thick, and of a reddish Cin&shy;
namon colour; it grows in a kind of little Cods, which they call <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Coque-Sigrues</term></f> in <f type="i">
Provence.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="raspberry(n)"><f type="i">Raspberries,</f> both <f type="i">Red</f> and <f type="i">White,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are propagated only by slips that sprout out of their
stocks every year in the <f type="i">Spring</f> time, and are sit to Replant the next <f type="i">Spring</f> after.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="reponce(n)" lexeme="field-radish(n)"><f type="i">Reponces,</f> or <f type="i">Field Radishes,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are multiplied only by Seed, and are a sort of little <f type="i">Radishes</f>
that are eaten in <f type="i">Sallats,</f> and grow without any pains in the Fields.</xpln><lemenote>"responces" was not found in OED.</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="rocambole(n)"><f type="i">Rocamboles,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are a sort of mild <f type="i">Garlick,</f> otherwise called <f type="i">Spanish Garlick,</f> which is mul&shy;
tiplied both by Cloves, and by Seed, which latter is about the bigness of ordinary <f type="i">Peas.</f></xpln><lemenote>"rocamboles" antedates earliest OED citation (1698); and "Spanish garlic" does so also (1707).</lemenote></wordentry>
</page>

<page sig="v1v">
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="rocket(n)"><f type="i">Rocket,</f> being one of the <f type="i">Sallat Furnitures,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is multiplied by Seed, which is extreme little,
and of a Cinnamon, or dark Tan colour.
</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="rosemary(n)"><f type="i">Rosemary</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a little very odoriferous Shrub, that is propagated by Seed or Branches that
have some portion of Root.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="rhubarb(n)"><f type="i">Rubarb</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is propagated only by Seed, which is pretty big, and triangular, the three An&shy;
gles being as thin as very thin Paper, and there being a thickness in the middle where
the Bud or Shoot is.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="rue(n)"><f type="i">Rue</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is multiplied by Seed, whose shape resembles that of a <f type="i">Cocks</f> Stone; it is of a black
colour and rugged; but yet we usually propagate it rather by its Layers and Cuttings,
than by its Seed.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="S"><alpha><f type="i">S.</f></alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="sage(n)">S<f type="i">Age</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is multiplied only by a kind of hooked slips that have a little Root.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="salsify(n)" lexeme="goat's-beard(n)"><f type="i">Salsifie,</f> or <f type="i">Goats-Beard,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="scorzonera(n)">the common sort is multiplied only by Seed, which is al&shy;
most like in all things to that of <f type="i">Scorzonera,</f> except in its colour, which is a little greyer;
it is of a very long oval figure, as if it were so many little Cods all over streaked, and
as 'twere engraven in the spaces between the streaks, which are pretty sharp pointed to&shy;
wards the ends.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="sampire(n)"><f type="i">Samphire</f> or <f type="i">Sampire:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Pierce-pierre.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Saracens(n)" lexeme="Turkey wheat(n)"><f type="i">Saracens Wheat,</f> or <f type="i">Turky Wheat,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a dark red Seed or Grain, about the bigness of an
ordinary <f type="i">Pea,</f> very smooth, round on one side, and a little flat on the other, where it is
fastned to its Spike or Ear.</xpln><lemenote>"Saracens" (for "Saracens wheat" or the French "bl&eacute;-sarrasin") not found in OED.</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="savory(n)"><f type="i">Savory</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is multiplied only by Seed, which is extraordinary small and round, slick,
and grey.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="scorzonera(n)" lexeme="salsify(n)"><f type="i">Scorzonera,</f> or <f type="i">Spanish Salsifie,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="pissabed(n)" lexeme="dandelion(n)">is propagated only by Seed, which is small, longish and
round withal, and of a white colour, and grows in a kind of Ball, mounted on the top
of the Stalk of the Plant, having its point garnished with a kind of Beard like that of
<f type="i">Pissabeds,</f> or <f type="i">Dandelions.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="celery(n)"><f type="i">Sellery:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Cellery.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="shalot(n)"><f type="i">Shalots</f> or <f type="i">Eschalots,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are multiplyed by Off-Sets or Kernels, which grow about the
foot of its Plant, and are about the bigness of a Filberd Nut.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="smallage(n)"><f type="i">Smallage</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is multiplyed only by Seed which is reddish, and pretty big, of a roundish
oval Figure, a little more full and rising on one side, than on the other, and is streaked
from one end to the other.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="sorrel(n)"><f type="i">Sorrel,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">as well the <f type="i">Lesser</f> one which is the common sort, as the <f type="i">Greater</f> one, are both
multiplyed only by Seed, which is very small, slick, and of a Triangular Oval Figure,
the ends of it being sharp and pointed, and being of an excellent dark Cinnamon Co&shy;
lour.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="round sorrel(n)"><f type="i">Round Sorrel,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is propagated only by Slips or Runners, so that out of one Tuft, we
may easily make several plants of it.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="French sorrel(n)" lexeme="wood-sorrel(n)"><f type="i">French</f> or <f type="i">Wood-Sorrel:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Alleluia.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="spearmint(n)"><f type="i">Spare-Mint:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Mint.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="spinach(n)"><f type="i">Spinage</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is multiplyed only by Seed, which is pretty big, and horned, or Triangu&shy;
lar on two Sides, having its corners very sharp pointed and prickly, and the other part
which is opposite to those pointed Horns, is like a Purse, of a Grayish colour.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="strawberry(n)"><f type="i">Straw-berry Plants,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="capron(n)">as well the white as the red, and those called <f type="i">Caprons,</f> are propaga&shy;
ted only by Runners, which are produced by a kind of Threads or Strings, which
springing out of the body of the Plant, and creeping along upon the Earth, easily e&shy;
nough take Root, at certain Joynts or Knots about a foot distance one from the other,
which knots coming to take Root, make new Plants, that in two or three Months time,
are fit to be transplanted, and they are placed three or four of them together, to make
what we call a Tuft.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="succory(n)"><f type="i">Succory:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Endive.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="sucker(n)" lexeme="artichoke(n)"><f type="i">Suckers</f> of <f type="i">Artichokes:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Artichokes.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="sharp trefoil(n)"><f type="i">Sharp Trefoil:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Alleluia.</f></xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="T"><alpha>T.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="thyme(n)">T<f type="i">Ime</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is multiplyed by Seed, which is very small, and sometimes we separate those
Plants or Stems of it that produce several rooted <f type="i">Slips</f> or <f type="i">Suckers,</f> to replant them
in Borders, for <f type="i">Time</f> is seldom planted otherwise.</xpln></wordentry>
</page>

<page sig="v2r">
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="trip-madam(n)"><f type="i">Tripe Madam</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is propagated both by Seed, and Cuttings or Slips, every Stem or Stock
of it producing several Arms, which being separated and replanted, easily take Root again.
The Seed of it is Gray, and Longish, and almost of the shape of <f type="i">Parsly</f> Seed; there
grows a great deal of it upon every Seed Stalk, which runs up one above another, like
those of Seed-<f type="i">Carrots, Parsnips,</f> &amp;c. there are seven or eight of them in a sort of little o&shy;
pen Cup, where they grow ripe after the falling of a yellow Flower, inclining to an
<f type="i">Olive</f> colour.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="turkey wheat(n)"><f type="i">Turkey Wheat:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Saracens Wheat.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="sharp trefoil(n)"><f type="i">Sharp Trefoil:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Alleluia.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="turnip(n)"><f type="i">Turneps</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are multiplyed only by Seed, which is almost like that of <f type="i">Cabbage.</f></xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="V"><alpha><f type="i">V.</f></alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="vine(n)" lexeme="muscat(n)"><f type="i">VInes</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="chasselas(n)" lexeme="muscat(n)" lexeme="bordelais(n)" lexeme="Corinthian(n)" lexeme="long muscat(n)" lexeme="passe-musque(n)">of what sort soever they be, whether <f type="i">White, Red,</f> or <f type="i">Black Muskat Chassela's,
Bourdelais, Corinthian,</f> or <f type="i">Long Muscat,</f> called otherwise <f type="i">Passe-Musqu&eacute;e,</f> &amp;c. are mul&shy;
tiplyed by Layers, by hooked or bent Slips, and especially Couched; and lastly, by <f type="i">Graft&shy;
ing Cleft-wise.</f></xpln><lemenote>Antedates earliest OED citation for "chasselas" (1699) and "black muscat" (1989); "bordelais", "Corinthian [muscat]", "long muscat", and "passe-musque" not found as separate entries in OED.</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="violet(n)"><f type="i">Violet Plants,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">as well of the <f type="i">Double</f> as <f type="i">Single</f> sort, and of what colour soever they be, though
they produce Seed in little reddish Shells or Husks, yet are multiplyed only by the Slips
they produce, each Plant or Stock of them growing insensibly into a great Tuft, which
is divided into several little ones, which being replanted, grow in time big enough to be
likewise divided into others.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="W"><alpha>W.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="wheat(n)">W<f type="i">heat:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Saracens</f> and <f type="i">Turkey Wheat.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="wormwood(n)"><f type="i">Worm wood,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is multiplyed by Seed, which is of a pretty odd Figure, being a little
bent inward in its smallest part, and a little open on the other end, which is bigger and
rounder, and upon which there is a little black spot. Its colour is yellowish at the bigger
end, and its sharper end inclines a little to black. Its Seed is seldom used, because it is
very difficult to fan or sift, being very light, and therefore when we have need of
propagating Worm-wood, we make use rather of its Cuttings and Layers, that are a lit&shy;
tle rooted.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="wood-sorrel(n)"><f type="i">Wood-Sorrel:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Alleluia.</f></xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</section>
</page>

<page sig="bb1r">
<section type="Culture of Plants">
Now to give you a particular account of the Culture that belongs to every several
sort of Plant, I must tell you, that this Culture consists, first, in observing the distances
they are to be placed at one from the other; second, in the Triming of such as need it;
</page>

<page sig="bb1v">
third, in planting them in that situation, and disposition, which they require;
fourth, in giving them those assistances which some of them have need of to bring them
to perfection, or which are convenient for them, whether it be by tying up, or wrapping
about, or Earthing up, or otherwise covering them, <f type="i">&amp;c.</f>

I begin in the <f type="i">ALPHABETICAL</f> Order.

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="A"><alpha>A.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="alexanders(n)">A<f type="i">LISANDERS:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Macedonian Parsley.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="alleluia(n)" lexeme="wood-sorrel(n)"><f type="i">Alleluia,</f> or <f type="i">Wood-Sorrel,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">when it grows old, grows into Tufts, and being
a Plant that grows in the Woods, and consequently that loves the shade, we
therefore plant it along by the sides of our <f type="i">Northern</f> Walls, at the distance of about one
foot between one plant and another; the more we strip it of its Leaves, which is one good
quality it has, the more fresh ones it shoots forth: It is enough to set it two Inches
into the Ground; it lasts three or four years without being renewed, and to renew it,
we need do no more than to separate or slip out the great Tufts of it into several little
ones, and replant them again immediately, which is to be done in the Months of
<f type="i">March</f> and <f type="i">April;</f> a little watering in very <f type="i">Hot</f> weather, and especially in sandy
Grounds, is a very great and welcome help to them.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="anice(n)" lexeme="fennel(n)"><f type="i">Anis</f> and <f type="i">Fennel</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are commonly sown pretty thin, either in furrows, or borders; their
Leaves are used in <f type="i">Sallets</f> among other Furnitures. They run to Seed towards the Month
of <f type="i">August,</f> and when their stalks are cut down, they shoot out new Leaves the next year
that are as good as the first, but however it is best to renew them every two years.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="orache(n)"><f type="i">Arrach, Orrach,</f> or <f type="i">Orage,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is propagated only by Seed, and is both one of the quickest
both in coming up, and in running to Seed, which latter it does at the very beginning of
<f type="i">June.</f> It is sown pretty thin, and to have good Seed of it, we must transplant some
plants of it in some separate place. The Leaf of this plant is very good both in pottage,
and in stuffings or farces; we use it almost as soon as it peeps out of the Earth, for it
passes away very quickly; and to have some the more early, we sow a little quantity of it
upon a <f type="i">Hot Bed:</f> It thrives well enough in all sorts of Grounds, but yet it grows always
fairer in good Grounds than in but indifferent ones.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="aromatic(n)"><f type="i">Aromatick</f> or sweet, or spicy Herbs</form> <xpln lang="en">such as are planted in Edgings of Borders, as <f type="i">Mar&shy;
joram, Time, Sage, Rosemary,</f> &amp;c. See their Culture under the several Titles of each of
those particular Herbs.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="artichoke(n)"><f type="i">Artichokes,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">as we have already elsewhere told you, are multiplied by their Eyes, Suck&shy;
ers, Slips, or Off-Sets, which every plant of them usually shoots out every year in the
Spring, round about its old Root, and which must be taken off as soon as they are grown
big enough, leaving only at each place three of the best and furthest distant one from the
other. For the planting them, we commonly make little Trenches, or Pits about half a
foot deep, and three foot distant one from another, and filled with Mold, and we place
two rows of them regulated by a line, in each Bed, which is to be full four foot broad,
and parted from the next Bed by a path-way of one full foot; these Trenches of
Pits are to be made at about half a foots distance from the edge of the Bed, and
Checquerwise one towards the other; we place two Slips in a right Line in each
space containing between Nine and Ten Inches in Length. We must renew them
once every three years at least, cut off their Leaves at the beginning of Win&shy;
ter, and cover them with long dry dung during all the very cold weather till the
end of <f type="i">March,</f> when we must uncover them, and slip them, if their Slips be yet big e&shy;
nough, or else stay three Weeks or a Month longer till they be, then we must labour
and move the Earth well about them, and dung them with the rottenest part of that
Dung that served them for a covering; we water them moderately once or twice a Week,
till about the end of <f type="i">May,</f> their Fruit begins to appear, and from that time we must wa&shy;
ter them plentifully, that is two or three times a Week, during the whole Summer, al&shy;
lowing half a Pitcher full of Water to each plant, and especially in Grounds naturally
dry; those planted in the Spring, should bring their Fruit to perfection in Autumn fol&shy;
lowing, if well watered, and they which do not, ought to yield their first Fruit in the
next Spring after, in case they be strong enough to resist the sharpness of the Winter.
<f type="i">Artichokes</f> have not only the hard weather, and excess of wet to fear, but they have the
Field Mice likewise for their Enemies, those mischievous little Animals gnawing their
Roots in the Winter-time, when they find nothing better in the Gardens, and for that
</page>

<page sig="bb2r">
reason, it's good to plant one Rank of <f type="i">Beet-Chards</f> between two Ranks of <f type="i">Artichokes,</f>
that the Field-Mice finding the Roots of these last the tendrer of the two, may fall up&shy;
on them instead of the others, as they never fail to do. There are three sorts of <f type="i">Arti&shy;
chokes, viz.</f> the green, or otherwise white ones, which are the most early, the violet
ones, whose Fruit is almost of a pyramidal Figure, and the red ones which are round,
and flat like the white ones. The two last sorts are the most delicious. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="chard(n)"><f type="i">Artichoke Chards.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Chards.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="asparagus(n)"><f type="i">Asparagus</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are sown at the beginning of the Spring like other Seeds, that is, they are
sown in some Bed well prepared; they must be sown indifferent thin, and raked with an
Iron Rake, to cover them with Earth. About a year after, if they be big enough, as
they will be, if the Ground be good, and well prepared; or if not, at least at two years
end, we must <emend err="tranplant">transplant</emend> them, which is to be done about the end of <f type="i">March,</f> and all the
Month of <f type="i">April;</f> and for that effect we must have Beds between three and four foot
broad, and separated one from the other; if it be in ordinary Ground, we dig these Beds
hollow with a good Spade, throwing up the Earth we take out of them upon the
Path-ways; and as to strong, heavy and moist Grounds, I would have them ordered
as I have done the <f type="i">Kitchen-Garden</f> at <f type="i">Versailles,</f> that is to say, I would not have the Beds in
them at all laid hollow, but on the contrary, raised and kept higher than the Path-ways,
too much wet being mortal to these Plants. <f type="i">Asparagus</f> thus sown shoot out Tufts of Roots
round about their Eye, or Mother Root; that is to say, round the place from whence all
their shoots are to Spring, which Roots spread between two Earths, and in order to
transplant them either into a hollow Bed, or a high raised Bed, we bestow a good tho&shy;
rough Tillage on the bottom of the Trench, and if the Ground be not very good, we
dung it a little, and afterward we plant two or three stocks of these young Plants, or&shy;
derly in ranks upon the Superficies of the Bed prepared for them, without needing to
trim the extremity of their Roots, or at least, but a very little, and if our intention be,
to force these <f type="i">Asparagus</f> by an artificial heat, when they are grown big enough, we place
them at a foot distance one from the other, and if they be to remain to grow after the
usual manner, we allow them at a foot and a half's distance, but in both Cases, we
place them Checquerwise, and when they are so placed, we cover them up again with
two or three Inches depth of Earth: if any of them fail to Spring up, we may reimplace
them with new ones two or three Months after, which is to be done in the same man&shy;
ner as we planted the others, only taking care to water the new planted ones sometimes
during the great heat, and to keep them always well weeded, and well dug about, or
else we mark out with little sticks the empty places, and stay till the Spring before
we fill them up again. Every year we cover the Bed with a little Earth taken off from
the Path-way, because instead of sinking, they always are rising by little and little: we
dung them moderately every two years, and let them shoot up the first three or four
years without gathering any, till we see them begin to grow pretty thick, and then we
may force as many as we please of them, or if not, we continue to gather of them every
year a crop for fifteen years, before we need to renew them. Every year about <f type="i">Martle&shy;
mas,</f> we cut down all their stems, every stock producing several stems, and take the seed
of the fairest of them for Seed, if we would have them come to bear at the time above-&shy;
mentioned. To draw them out of their <f type="i">Nursery-Beds,</f> we use an Iron Fork, the <f type="i">Spade</f> be&shy;
ing too dangerous for that work, because it would cut, and hurt those little
Plants.

We must not fail every Year at the latter end of <f type="i">March,</f> or beginning of <f type="i">April,</f>
that is, before the <f type="i">Asparagus</f> begin to sprout naturally, to bestow a small dressing or
stirring of the Ground about three or four Inches deep, on every Bed, taking care not
to let the <f type="i">Spade</f> go so deep, as to hurt the Plants; which small dressing serves, both to
kill the Weeds and to render the Superficies of the Earth loose, and thereby not only the
better to dispose it to drink up the Rain, and the <f type="i">May-dew</f> that nourishes the Stocks, but
likewise to facilitate the passage of the <f type="i">Asparagus</f> in sprouting. The particular and most
dreadful Enemies of <f type="i">Asparagus</f> are a sort of little Fleas, that fasten upon their shoots,
make them miscarry, and hinder them from thriving; they are most troublesome in ve&shy;
ry hot and dry years, not appearing at all in other years; there has been no Remedy
found yet against this mischief.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="B"><alpha>B.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="balm(n)">B<f type="i">Alm,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">called in <f type="i">French <term lang="fr">Melisse</term>,</f> is an Odoriferous Herb, whose Leaf when tender, makes
a part of Sallet-Furnitures. It is multiplyed both by Seed, and by rooted Branches,
like <f type="i">Lavender, Time, Hyssop,</f> &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>
</page>

<page sig="bb2v">
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="basil(n)"><f type="i">Basil</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is an annual Plant, that is very delicate. We seldom sow it but upon <f type="i">Hot Beds,</f>
and not in open Ground, as we do <f type="i">Purslain, Lettuce,</f> &amp;c. We begin to sow some in
that manner at the very beginning of <f type="i">February,</f> and we may continue so to do the whole
year. Its tender Leaves are mixed in a small quantity, with the Furnitures of <f type="i">Sallets,</f>
among which, they make an agreeable perfume; It is likewise used in <f type="i">Ragou's,</f> especially
dry ones, for which reason, we take care to keep some for <f type="i">Winter.</f> We gather its Seed
in the Month of <f type="i">August;</f> and usually to make it run to Seed, we transplant it in the
Month of <f type="i">May,</f> either in Pots, or <f type="i">Beds.</f> There are several sorts of it, but that which
bears the biggest Leaves, and especially if they incline to a <f type="i">Violet Colour,</f> and that which
bears the least Leaves, are the two most curious; that which produces midling ones,
being the ordinary or Common sort. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bay(n)" lexeme="bay tree(n)">The Common <f type="i">Bays,</f> or <f type="i">Bay-Tree,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a shurb of no very great use in our <f type="i">Gardens,</f> and
therefore it is enough to have some few Plants of it in some well sheltered place, to gather
some Leaves of them when occasion requires.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bean(n)"><f type="i">Beans,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="kidney bean(n)" lexeme="French bean(n)" lexeme="hastings(n)">as well those of the Common, and <f type="i">Garden</f> sort, as those called <f type="i">Kidney-Beans,</f>
and <f type="i">French-Beans,</f> and in <f type="i">French, <term lang="fr">Aric&ocirc;s</term>,</f> are sown in open Ground, and grow not other&shy;
wise; The <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Aric&ocirc;</term> French,</f> or <f type="i">Kidney-Beans,</f> are sown about the latter end of <f type="i">April,</f> and all
the Month of <f type="i">May,</f> and are very sensible of the <f type="i">Frost;</f> The Common <f type="i">Garden Beans</f>
are sown at the same time with <f type="i">Hasting-Peas,</f> both in <f type="i">November,</f> and in <f type="i">Febru&shy;
ary.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="hotbed(n)"><f type="i">Hot Beds.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See the Works in <f type="i">November.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="rave(n)" lexeme="beet-rave(n)"><f type="i">Bete-raves,</f> or <f type="i">Red Beet-Roots,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are annual Plants propagated only by Seed, and are
seldom transplanted. They are sown in the Month of <f type="i">March,</f> either in <f type="i">Beds</f> or Borders.
They must be sown very thin, or at least if they come up too thick they must be very
much thinned, or else they will not grow so fair and large as they should be. They
require a very good, and well prepared Ground: They are the best that have the <f type="i">Reddest</f>
substance and the <f type="i">Reddest</f> Tops: They are not good to spend till towards the end of
<f type="i">Autumn,</f> and all the <f type="i">Winter</f> Season. To have Seed of them, we transplant in <f type="i">March</f> some
of the last years Roots that we have preserved from the <f type="i">Frost,</f> their Seed is gathered in the
Months of <f type="i">August</f> and <f type="i">September.</f></xpln><lemenote>"beet-rave" antedates the earliest OED citation (1736).</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="chard(n)"><f type="i">White,</f> or <f type="i">Chard-Beets:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Chards.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="borage(n)"><f type="i">Borage</f> and <f type="i">Bugloss,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">grow and are to be ordered in the same manner as <f type="i">Arrach,</f> only
they come not up so vigorously. We sow of them several times in the same <f type="i">Summer,</f> because
their Leaves, in which consists all their excellence, are good only whilst they are tender,
that is, while they are young. Their little <f type="i">Violet Colour'd Flowers</f> serve to adorn <f type="i">Sallets.</f>
Their Seed falls assoon as 'tis ripe, and therefore must be carefully watch'd: The surest
way is to cut down the stalks, and lay them a drying in the <f type="i">Sun,</f> assoon as ever the Seeds
begin to ripen, and by that means we shall lose but very few.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bordelais(n)"><f type="i">Bourdelai's,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="verjuice(n)">otherwise called <f type="i">Verjuice,</f> as well the <f type="i">White,</f> as <f type="i">Red</f> sort, is a kind of <f type="i">Vine</f>
which is pruned, and slipt, or layed, and graffed and planted as other <f type="i">Vines</f> are in the
Months of <f type="i">January, February,</f> and <f type="i">March.</f> Care must be taken to tie up its Branches,
either to props, or some sort of trail, about the middle of <f type="i">June</f> at latest, or else the
wind destroys it quite: We must also pick and pluck off the weak and unprofitable
Branches of these <f type="i">Vines;</f> and when we prune them, 'tis enough to leave two, three,
or four fair Branches at most upon each stock, and to keep them not above three or four
buds long, every one of which usually shoots forth one bearing Branch, with three or four
fair Bunches of <f type="i">Grapes</f> upon each Branch. My practice is in all sorts of <f type="i">Vines,</f> but par&shy;
ticularly in the <f type="i">Muscat</f> or <f type="i">Musked</f> sorts, to keep the lower Branches shorter by two buds,
than the highest, to keep the Plant always low, when I would not have them mount up
upon a Trail.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="buck's horn(n)"><f type="i">Buckshorn-Sallet:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Hartshorn-Sallet.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="burnet(n)"><f type="i">Burnet,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="pimpernel(n)">called in <f type="i">French <term lang="fr">Pimprenelle</term>,</f> or <f type="i">Pimpernelle,</f> is a very Common and ordinary
<f type="i">Sallet</f> furniture, which is seldom sown but in the <f type="i">Spring,</f> and is sown thick either in <f type="i">Beds</f> or
Borders.

It often Springs afresh after cutting, of which the youngest shoots must be chosen for
<f type="i">Sallets,</f> the Leaves that are any thing old being too tough. It does it a great deal of
good to water it in <f type="i">Summer.</f> There is but one sort of it whose Seed is gathered at the end
of <f type="i">Summer.</f></xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</page>

<page sig="bb3r">
<wordgroup1 type="a" object="C"><alpha>C.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="cabbage(n)">C<f type="i">Abbages</f> of all sorts of <f type="i">Kitchen-Plants</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="white cabbage(n)" lexeme="curled cabbage(n)" lexeme="Milan cabbage(n)" lexeme="cauliflower(n)">take Root again the easiliest when transplanted,
as they are likewise the most known, and most used of any in our whole <f type="i">Gardens.</f>
They are multiplied by Seed, and are of several sorts and Seasons. There are some called
<f type="i">White,</f> or headed <f type="i">Cabbages,</f> which are for the service of the latter end of <f type="i">Summer,</f> and for
<f type="i">Autumn.</f> There are some Curled, called <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Pancaliers</term>,</f> and <f type="i">Milan Cabbages,</f> which produce
small headed <f type="i">Cabbages,</f> for <f type="i">Winter;</f> there are some of a <f type="i">Red</f> or <f type="i">Violet Colour;</f> and some
called long sided <f type="i">Cabbages,</f> whereof some are Bright or <f type="i">White,</f> and very delicate, ripe in
<f type="i">Vintage</f> time, and others <f type="i">Green,</f> and are not very good till they be <f type="i">Frost-</f>bitten. Lastly,
there are some called <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Choux Fleurs</term>,</f> i. e. <f type="i">Cabbage Flowers,</f> and by the English <f type="i">Collyflowers,</f>
which are the most noble and valuable of them all, and are not used in pottage, but in
choice intermesses; they cannot endure the <f type="i">Frost,</f> and therefore assoon as they begin to
form their heads, they must be covered with their Leaves tied up for that end over
them, with Straw bands, to guard them from the insults of the Cold that spoil and rot
them. They are for our <f type="i">Winter</f> spending, and must be sheltred in the <f type="i">Green</f> house or
<f type="i">Conservatory,</f> whither they must be carried and there planted with a turf of their old Earth,
about them, where they commonly are used to perfect the full growth of their heads.
All other <f type="i">Cabbage-Plants</f> yield Seed in <f type="i">France,</f> but only these, whose Seed we are fain to
have brought up from the <f type="i">Eastern</f> Countries, which makes them ordinarily very dear. To
make <f type="i">Cabbages</f> run to Seed, we use every year either in <f type="i">Autumn</f> or <f type="i">Spring</f> to transplant
some of the best and fairest of them, which run to Seed in the Months of <f type="i">May</f> and <f type="i">June,</f>
that is gathered in <f type="i">July</f> and <f type="i">August.</f>

You are by the way to remark two things; The first is, that all thick Plants that run
to Seed, and grow pretty high, as <f type="i">Cabbage, Leeks, Ciboules, Onions, Red Beet-Roots, Car&shy;
rots, Parsnips, Cellery,</f> &amp;c. must be supported either with upright props, or cross
sticks, to hinder the wind from breaking down their stems before the Seed be
Ripe.

The second is, that we seldom stay to let any Seeds dry upon their Plants as they
stand, it being enough to let them only Ripen, when we cut down their stems, and lay
them to dry upon some Cloth, after which, we beat them out, and fan and cleanse them
and lay them up when they are fully dry: And thus we do with the Seeds of <f type="i">Cresses,
Chervil, Parsley, Radishes, Borage, Bugloss,</f> &amp;c.</xpln><lemenote>"Milan cabbage" not found in OED.</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="caper(n)">Ordinary <f type="i">Capers</f></form> <xpln lang="en">grow upon a sort of small Shrub that is raised in niches made purposely
in well exposed Walls, for that end, which are filled with Earth to nourish the Plants;
and every year in the <f type="i">Spring</f> we prune their Branches, which afterwards shoot out buttons
or swelling buds, which are pickled up in Vinegar to be used in <f type="i">Winter,</f> either in <f type="i">Sallets,</f>
or in pottage.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="capucine caper(n)"><f type="i">Capucine-Capers,</f> or <f type="i">Nasturces,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are annual Plants which are usually sown in <f type="i">Hot Beds,</f>
in the Month of <f type="i">March,</f> and transplanted again in the naked Earth along by some Walls, or
at the foot of some <f type="i">Trees,</f> where their mounting stalks which are but weak, and grow pretty
high may take some hold, to support themselves. They are also planted in Pots, and
Boxes, in which some sticks are set up to support their stalks. Their Buttons or round
Buds before they open, are good to pickle in Vinegar. Their <f type="i">Flower</f> is pretty large of
an <f type="i">Orange Colour,</f> and very agreeable. They must be carefully watered in the <f type="i">Summer,</f> to
make them shoot vigorously, and so long time as they should. Their Seed falls to the
Earth assoon as ever 'tis ripe, as well as that of <f type="i">Borage</f> and <f type="i">Bugloss,</f> and therefore must be
carefully gathered up.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="capron(n)" lexeme="strawberry(n)"><f type="i">Caprons,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are a sort of large <f type="i">Straw-berries,</f> not over delicate, which ripen at the
same time as those of the better kind. Their Leaves are extraordinary large, velveted
and of a darkish <f type="i">Green Colour.</f> They are little to be prized, and are found in the Woods
as other <f type="i">Straw-berries</f> are.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="i" lexeme="cardoon(n)">Spanish Cardons</f> or <f type="i">Cardoons,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">grow only from Seed. They are sown at two several
times.

The first is commonly about the middle, or latter end of <f type="i">April,</f> and the second, at
the beginning of <f type="i">May.</f>

They must be sown in good and well prepared Ground, and in little Trenches or
pits a full foot wide, and about six Inches deep, filled with Mold. We make <f type="i">Beds</f> of four
or five foot wide, in order to place in them two ranks of those little Trenches or pits
checker-wise. We put five or six Seeds in every hole, with intention to let but two or
three of them grow, if they all come up, taking away those that are over and above that
</page>

<page sig="bb3v">
number, either to throw away, or to new stock those places where there perhaps are
none come up, or where we may have sown some few upon a <f type="i">Hot Bed</f> for that intention.
And if in fifteen or twenty days we do not see the Seed come up, we should uncover
them, to see whether they be rotten, or begin to sprout, that so we may fill up their
places with new ones in case of need. The Seeds of the first sowing are generally
three weeks coming up, and those of the second fifteen days. <f type="i">Cardons</f> must not be sown
before the middle of <f type="i">April,</f> for fear they should grow too big, and run to Seed in <f type="i">August</f>
and <f type="i">September,</f> and then they are not good. Great care must be taken to water them
well; and when towards the end of <f type="i">October,</f> we have a mind to whiten them, we take
the advantage of some dry day, first to tie up all their Leaves with two or three bands,
and some days after, we cover them quite up with Straw or dry Litter well twisted
about them, so that the Air may not penetrate to come at them, except it be at the very
top, which we leave open.

These <f type="i">Cardoon</f> Plants thus wrapt up, whiten in about fifteen days or three weeks, and
grow fit to Eat. We make an end of tying up, and wrapping, or covering all that
we have in our <f type="i">Gardens,</f> when we perceive the <f type="i">Winter</f> approach, and then we take
them up with the Earth about them, to transplant them in our <f type="i">Green</f> House, or <f type="i">Con&shy;
servatory:</f> Some of those Plants are good to transplant in the naked Earth in the
following <f type="i">Spring,</f> to run to Seed in <f type="i">June,</f> or <f type="i">July,</f> or else some Plants of them
tied up in their first places, will serve for that three or four times together.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="carrot(n)"><f type="i">Carrots</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are a sort of Root, whereof some are <f type="i">White,</f> and others <f type="i">Yellow,</f> that grow only
from Seed, and require the same care and ordering which we have already described
under the head of <f type="i">Red Beet-Roots.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="celery(n)"><f type="i">Cellery,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a sort of <f type="i">Sallet</f> produced by Seed, and is not good but at the end of <f type="i">Autumn,</f>
and during the <f type="i">Winter</f> Season. We sow of it two several times, to be supplied with
it so much the longer, because that which has been long sown, easily runs to Seed, and
grows hard. We sow it then the first time upon <f type="i">Hot Beds</f> in the beginning of <f type="i">April,</f>
and because its Seed is so extream small, we cannot help sowing it too thick, so that if
we be not careful to thin it, and crop it in time, to make it grow to some strength and
bigness before we transplant it, it warps and flags its head too much, and grows weak,
and shoots its Leaves straglingly outward, instead of producing store of them from the
middle of its stock. The surest way is to transplant it in a <f type="i">Nursery Bed,</f> placing the
Plants two or three Inches one from another, for which we make holes with our fingers
only; we transplant that which comes of the first sowing at the beginning of <f type="i">June,</f>
and sow our second sowing, at the latter end of <f type="i">May,</f> or beginning of <f type="i">June,</f> but 'tis in
open <f type="i">Beds,</f> and we take the same care to thin, crop, and transplant this, as we did that
of the first sowing, but we must plant more of it the second time, than at the first.
There are two ways of transplanting it; the one is in a Pit or Trench one full spit deep,
and between three and four foot broad, in order to place in it three or four ranks of these
Plants at the distance of one foot from one another: This way of making hollow <f type="i">Beds</f>
Earth up our <f type="i">Cellery</f> in, is good only in dry Grounds, wet ones being too apt to rot,
it. The second way of transplanting it, is in plain <f type="i">Beds</f> that are not made hollow, and at
the same distances as in the other, taking care in both sorts of <f type="i">Beds</f> to water them ex&shy;
treamly in <f type="i">Summer</f> time, its chief goodness consisting in being tender, as well as in being
very <f type="i">White.</f> Watering contributes to the first kind of goodness, and for the second, you
are to observe, that to <f type="i">Whiten Cellery,</f> we begin at first to tie it with two bands when it
is big enough, chusing dry weather for that effect, and afterward we Earth our <f type="i">Cellery</f>
Plants quite up, with Earth taken off the high raised path-ways, or else cover it all
over with a good quantity of long dry Dung, or dry Leaves, as we do <f type="i">Cardoons. Cellery</f>
so Earthed up with dry Earth, or Clothed with long dry Dung, or dry Leaves, to the
very top of its Leaves, <f type="i">Whitens</f> in three weeks or a Month, and because when 'tis <f type="i">Whi&shy;
ted,</f> it rots as it stands, if it be not presently eaten, by consequence, we are not to
Earth it up, or cover it with Dung, but in such proportion as we are able to spend out
of hand; there needs no other precaution to be used to it so long as it does not freeze;
but as soon as ever it begins to set to freeze, we must then cover up our <f type="i">Cellery</f> quite over
head and ears, for a hard <f type="i">Frost</f> spoils it presently. And that we may the more easily
cover it, after we have first tied it up with two or three bands, we take it up with the
Earth about it, at the beginning of <f type="i">Winter,</f> and plant it in another <f type="i">Bed,</f> setting the Plants
as close as we can one to another, and then there needs much less stuff to cover them,
than when they are left standing in their old places at such great distances asunder. The
way to raise Seed from them, is, to transplant some Plants of them in some by-place, after
<f type="i">Winter</f> is past, which will not fail to run to Seed in the Month of <f type="i">August,</f> we know but
one sort of it.</xpln></wordentry>
</page>

<page sig="bb4r">
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="chard(n)" lexeme="coston(n)"><f type="i">Chards</f> of <f type="i">Artichokes,</f> otherwise called <f type="i">Costons,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are the Leaves of fair <f type="i">Artichoke</f> Plants
tied, and wrapt up with Straw in Autumn and Winter, which being covered up all o&shy;
ver but at their very top, with Straw, grow white, and by that means, lose a little of
their bitterness, so that when they are boiled, they are served up like true <f type="i">Spanish Cardons,</f>
but after all, are not so good, and besides the Plants often rot and perish whilst we are
whiting them.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="chard-beet(n)"><f type="i">Chard-Beets</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are Plants of white <f type="i">Beets</f> transplanted in a well prepared Bed, at the di&shy;
stance of a full foot one from the other, which produce great Tops, that in the middle
have a large white, and thick downy Cotton-like Main shoot, and that downy Cotton-&shy;
like shoot is the true <f type="i">Chard</f> used in Pottages and Intermesses. After we have sown white
<f type="i">Beets</f> upon <f type="i">Hot Beds,</f> or in the naked Earth, in the Month of <f type="i">March,</f> we transplant that
which is yellowest in <f type="i">Beds</f> purposely prepared, and by taking care to water them well during
the Summer, they grow big and strong enough to resist the hard winter cold, provided
care be taken to cover them with long dry Dung, just as we do <f type="i">Artichokes.</f> They are
likewise well placed, when two Ranks of them are transplanted between two Ranks of
<f type="i">Artichokes.</f> We uncover them in <f type="i">April,</f> and dress the Earth about them, and give them
careful attendance, and by the means of this diligent Culture, they produce those fine
<f type="i">Chards</f> we have in the <f type="i">Rogation</f> Season, and in the Months of <f type="i">May</f> and <f type="i">June;</f> in fine,
they run to Seed, which we gather in the Months of <f type="i">July</f> and <f type="i">August,</f> to sow in the
following Spring.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="chasselas(n)">The <f type="i">Chassela's</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a very good and sweet sort of Grape, of which there are two kinds,
white and Red, and this latter is very scarce and rare, but the other very common. It
requires the good Expositions of the <f type="i">South, East</f> and <f type="i">West,</f> to be so much the yellower,
the more firm and crackling, the better; It is of all Grapes that which keeps lon&shy;
gest, if it be not suffered to grow too ripe upon the Vine before it be gathered. Its
Culture which consists in pruning it, is the same with that of the <f type="i">Bourdelais</f> or Verjuice
Grape.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="chervil(n)">Musked <f type="i">Chervil</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is one of our Sallet-Furnitures, and at the beginning of the Spring,
whilst its Leaves are young and tender, it is agreeable, and proper to contribute towards
the giving a perfuming Relish, but they are to be used no longer when they are old and
tough. It remains several Years in its place without being spoiled by the Frost, so that
its Stock grows pretty big and high: it runs to Seed towards the Month of <f type="i">June,</f> and by
that is multiplyed.</xpln><lemenote>"musked chervil" not found in OED.</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="chervil(n)">Ordinary <f type="i">Chervil</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is an annual Plant, or rather a plant of few Months, which serves
for many Uses, and especially in Sallets, when it is young and tender, and therefore we
ought to see a little of it every Month proportionably to the occasions we have for it,
and to the quantity of Ground we have. It runs very easily to Seed, and if we have
some of it betimes, we must sow it about the end of <f type="i">Autumn,</f> and doubtless we shall have
the Seed quite ripe towards the middle of <f type="i">June</f> following; we cut down the stalks as
soon as it begins to grow yellow, and beat it out as we do that of other Plants.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr"><f type="i">Chicons</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="chibol(n)">are a sort of <f type="i">Lettuces</f> to tie up; see their Culture under <f type="i">Lettuces. Cibouls</f> or
<f type="i">Chibouls,</f> properly speaking, are but <f type="i">Onions</f> that are degenerated, and of which Nature has
as 'twere miscarried, that is to say, <f type="i">Onions</f> that instead of producing a thick Root in the
Earth, and one single stem, produces but a small Root, and several Stems, or upright
Shoots, and those which produce most of them, are most esteemed, which are the sort of
which we should be most careful to preserve Seed, and which if planted in <f type="i">March</f> will
yield us Seed fit to gather in <f type="i">August.</f> We sow <f type="i">Cibouls</f> almost every Month in the Year,
except in very hard Weather, when the Earth cannot be cultivated; their Seed is so per&shy;
fectly like that of <f type="i">Onions,</f> that they cannot be distinguisht one from the other, but the
former never recover so as to produce <f type="i">Onions,</f> and particularly those we pluck up out of
the <f type="i">Onion</f> Beds, which are sown too thick, and must be thin'd, that those which are left,
may grow the bigger. We thin our <f type="i">Cibouls</f> also for the same, and we transplant some
which prosper very well, and grow big when they are so transplanted. It is conveni&shy;
ent sometimes to water our <f type="i">Ciboul Beds</f> in <f type="i">Summers</f> that prove extraordinary dry, and un&shy;
less in such cases, they will not need watering, but however they must be always planted
in good Earth.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="chive(n)" lexeme="appetite(n)">English <f type="i">Cives,</f> otherwise called <f type="i">Appetites,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are multiplied by producing thick Tufts, which
are slipt out and separated into many little ones, and are transplanted nine or ten Inches
asunder, either in Borders or <f type="i">Beds;</f> they require pretty good Ground, with which if they
be accommodated, they will last three or four years without removing, without needing
any great culture; it being enough to keep them well weeded, and to water them some&shy;
</page>

<page sig="bb4v">
times during the great heat. It is their Leaves only, that are used for one of the <f type="i">Sallet</f>
Furnitures.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="pompion(n)" lexeme="citrul(n)" lexeme="pumpkin(n)" lexeme="potiron(n)"><f type="i">Citrulls</f> or ordinary <f type="i">Pompions, Pumpions,</f> or <f type="i">Pumkins,</f> and <f type="i">Potirons,</f> or flat <f type="i">Pumpions,</f></form>
<xpln lang="en">as every body knows, are the biggest productions the Earth brings forth in our <f type="i">Climates,</f>
for whose culture there is little to be done; usually we sow them in <f type="i">Hot Beds</f> towards the
middle of <f type="i">March,</f> that being the only way to preserve and multiply them, and at the end
of <f type="i">April,</f> we take them up with the Earth about them, to transplant them in holes
made for that purpose, of about two foot diameter, and one foot deep, and two <f type="i">Toises</f> or
<f type="i">Fathoms</f> asunder one from the other, which are filled with mold; when their <f type="i">Vines</f> begin
