to grow five or six foot long, which happens about the beginning of <f type="i">June,</f> we throw
upon them in the middle of that length some shovels full of Earth, both to prevent their
being broken by the winds driving them to and fro, and to make them take root at
the place so covered, by which means the <f type="i">Fruit</f> that grows beyond that part, will be
the better nourished and consequently grow the bigger: There are two sorts of <f type="i">Citruls</f>
or <f type="i">Pumpions,</f> the <f type="i">Green,</f> and the <f type="i">Whitish</f> ones, but neither of them are fit to be gathered
till they be Augusted, that is till they be grown <f type="i">Yellow,</f> and their skin grown tough
enough to resist ones nail. We keep of them in our store-houses, till about the middle of
<f type="i">Lent,</f> when they have been seasonably gather'd, and well defended from the <f type="i">Cold:</f>
All sorts of situation in the open Air agree with them well enough, but yet they which
are well exposed ripen sooner than the others; we trim nothing off from them, but
only content our selves with watering them sometimes when the <f type="i">Summers</f> are excessive
dry. Their Seed is found in their <f type="i">Bellies.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="colewort(n)" lexeme="cauliflower(n)"><f type="i">Coleworts</f> and <f type="i">Collyflowers</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are included under <f type="i">Cabbage.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="coston(n)" lexeme="artichoke(n)"><f type="i">Costons</f> of <f type="i">Artichokes:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Chards.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="cress(n)"><f type="i">Garden Cresses,</f> is one of the little <f type="i">Sallet</f> Furnitures, and</form> <xpln lang="en">is a Plant that lasts but a little
while. We sow of it every Month as we do <f type="i">Chervil,</f> that we may have always some
of it that is tender; and we sow it very thick. It is propagated only by Seed, which it
is very apt to run to, and which we begin to gather at the end of <f type="i">June,</f> cutting down
the stalks in order to dry them, and beat out the Seeds and winnow them as we do
those of other Plants, assoon as we perceive any of them to ripen.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="cucumber(n)"><f type="i">Cucumber:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See their culture under the head of <f type="i">Melons,</f> and <f type="i">Musk-Melons.</f> It is to be
observed, that a <f type="i">Cucumber</f> Plant yields a great quantity of <f type="i">Fruit,</f> and for a long time,
when 'tis well cultivated, and especially when 'tis well watered.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="currant(n)" lexeme="gooseberry(n)" lexeme="Dutch gooseberry(n)"><f type="i">Currans</f> and <f type="i">Goose berries,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">both being comprehended under the <f type="i">French</f> name <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Groseilles</term>,</f>
both the <f type="i">Red</f> and <f type="i">White,</f> or <f type="i">Pearled</f> sort, termed in <f type="i">English, Currans,</f> and the prickly sort,
called in <f type="i">English, Dutch Goose-berries,</f> are kinds of little <f type="i">Fruit</f> shrubs, which yield a great
deal of <f type="i">Fruit.</f> They produce round about their old stock, a great number of rooted
suckers or slips, which serve to propagate them, besides which their Branches and espe&shy;
cially the young ones that are cut off from them, take root easily. They are planted in
the Month of <f type="i">March,</f> at the distance of at least six good foot one from the other, either
in whole <f type="i">Beds,</f> or squares, or in the void spaces between the <f type="i">Dwarf-Trees</f> which are usually
planted about the squares of <f type="i">Kitchen</f> or <f type="i">Fruit-Gardens.</f> Both of them delight in a Ground
that is a little moist, the better to enable them to produce thick shoots, and consequently
good <f type="i">Fruit.</f>

The <f type="i">Red</f> and <f type="i">Pearled,</f> or <f type="i">White</f> sort, called in <f type="i">English Currans</f> produce Bunches, which
are Ripe in <f type="i">July,</f> but the prickly ones, named in <f type="i">English, Goose berries,</f> produce none, but
bear their <f type="i">Fruit</f> upon single stalks all along the young Branches of the preceeding years
growth, and that at the place of every one of the Eyes or Buds of that Branch. The
<f type="i">Fruit</f> of this latter is used particularly in <f type="i">March</f> and <f type="i">April,</f> in <f type="i">Comp&ocirc;tes</f> or wet sweet-meats,
and sauces, for which uses it must be very <f type="i">Green,</f> for when it is Ripe, it grows too
soft and flat. The culture that is most proper to be used to both <f type="i">Currans</f> and <f type="i">Goose-berries,</f>
and especially to the <f type="i">Currans,</f> consists in cutting away all their old wood, and preserving
only that of one and two years growth: for a confused mixture of one with the other,
is not only very indisagreeable and pernicious, but the old Branches will bear nothing
but very small <f type="i">Fruit,</f> till at last they quite degenerate, so that they will bear none but small,
common, and very crabbed sowre <f type="i">Currans</f> or <f type="i">Goose-berries,</f> and assoon as the old stocks
have done bearing any longer either fair Branches or good <f type="i">Fruit,</f> we should take a Re&shy;
solution utterly to grub them up, after we have first raised a plantation of new ones in
some other choice fresh piece of Ground, to supply their places; for a <f type="i">Garden</f> ought by
no means to be without fair <f type="i">Currans</f> and <f type="i">Goose-berries,</f> and assoon as ever the new ones begin
to bear, we are to destroy the old ones, which make but a very unsightly figure in a
<f type="i">Garden.</f></xpln><lemenote>"Dutch gooseberry" not found in OED.</lemenote></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</page>

<page sig="cc1r">
<wordgroup1 type="a" object="D"><alpha>D.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="sharp dock(n)" lexeme="dock-sorrel(n)" lexeme="patience(n)">SHarp <f type="i">Dock,</f> or <f type="i">Dock-Sorrel,</f> or <f type="i">Patience,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">properly speaking, is but a sort of very great
or large <f type="i">Sorrel,</f> which is very sower. We content our selves only with some borders,
or perhaps, some one single <f type="i">Bed</f> of it, to have some of its Leaves to mix now and
then among our <f type="i">Sorrel.</f> The manner of raising it is the same we practise with <f type="i">Sor&shy;
rel.</f></xpln><lemenote>"dock-sorrel" antedates earliest citation in OED (1886).</lemenote></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="E"><alpha>E.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="endive(n)"><f type="i">ENdive</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="succory(n)" lexeme="wild endive(n)">is a sort of very good annual Plant used in <f type="i">Sallets,</f> and in our pottage in the
<f type="i">Autumn</f> and <f type="i">Winter</f> Seasons, provided it be well whitened, and consequently tender
and delicate; it is multiplied only by Seed. There is the Common or <f type="i">Garden Endive,</f>
and wild <f type="i">Endive,</f> called also <f type="i">Succory,</f> the common name in <f type="i">French</f> to them both. The
Common <f type="i">Endive</f> is of several kinds, <f type="i">viz.</f> The <f type="i">White,</f> which is the most delicate, and
the <f type="i">Green</f> sort which is the most rustical, and best able to resist the <f type="i">Cold,</f> as likewise the
Curled sort, and that which is not Curled.

All sorts of them agree tolerably well with all kinds of Ground. We seldom begin to
sow any of them till towards the middle of <f type="i">May,</f> and then they must be sown very thin,
or be very much thinned, <emend err="aftewards">afterwards</emend> in order to be whitened in the places where they
first grow, without transplanting, and we also sow but a little quantity of them at once,
because they are too apt to run to Seed: The season for sowing a greater quantity of
them is at the latter end of <f type="i">June,</f> and during the whole Month of <f type="i">July,</f> in order to have
some good for spending in <f type="i">September,</f> and we afterwards sow a great deal of it again in
<f type="i">August,</f> that we may have a sufficient provision of it to supply us all the rest of <f type="i">Autumn,</f>
and the first part of the <f type="i">Winter.</f> When our <f type="i">Endive</f> comes up too thick, we cut it, or else
pull up some of it, to thin it, that the rest may grow big enough to be transplanted;
and when we transplant any of it in <f type="i">Summer</f> time, it must be placed at the distance of a
large foot between Plant and Plant; we usually make great <f type="i">Beds</f> of five or six foot
broad, in order to transplant them afterwards in rows markt out strait with a cord. This
Plant requires great and frequent waterings; and when 'tis big enough we must go to
work to whiten it, for which effect, we tie it up with two or three bands according as
its height requires; and being so tied, it whitens in fifteen, or twenty days: But because
it is very apprehensive of the <f type="i">Frost,</f> therefore assoon as ever the <f type="i">Cold</f> begins to come on,
we cover it with long dry Dung, whether it be tied up or no: At the end of <f type="i">September,</f>
we plant the stocks of it pretty near together because then it grows neither so high,
nor spreads so much as in <f type="i">Summer:</f> And if we can save any Plants of it in <f type="i">Winter,</f> we
must transplant them again in the <f type="i">Spring</f> in order to produce Seed that may have sufficient
time to ripen. Those persons that have a good <f type="i">Conservatory</f> or <f type="i">Green</f> house, will do
well to house it up there, but they which have none must be content to cover it up
well with a good quantity of long dry Dung, so that the <f type="i">Frost</f> may not come at
it.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="wild endive(n)">Wild <f type="i">Endive,</f> or <f type="i">Succory</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is sown at the very beginning of the Month of <f type="i">March,</f> and
that pretty thick, and in Ground well prepared. We endeavour to fortifie it, and make
it grow big all <f type="i">Summer,</f> by watering, and cropping it that it may be fit to whiten in
<f type="i">Winter.</f>

There are some People that will eat it <f type="i">Green</f> in <f type="i">Sallets</f> though it be never so bitter, but
commonly they rather desire it whitened: And to whiten it, we cover it up with a
great deal of long Dung, after we have first cut it close to the Earth, by which means,
it been forced to spring up in obscurity, and shaded from all light, its young shoots
grow <f type="i">White</f> and tender. The neatest way is by the interposition of some props crossing
from side to side, to keep the Dung from touching it, since it shoots up in the same
manner under such a hollow covering as under a close one, so that care be taken, so well
to stop up passages on all sides, that no light or Air at all get in. Being thus ordered,
its shoots are much cleanlier, and relish not so much of the Dung. They which have
<f type="i">Conservatories</f> may transplant some of it thither in <f type="i">Winter,</f> it sprouting well enough there,
when it is but a little obscurely placed. When it is <f type="i">Green</f> it endures the <f type="i">Frost</f> well enough,
and at the very latter end of <f type="i">May,</f> it runs to Seed. Many People eat its young shoots in
<f type="i">Sallets</f> when they are young and tender.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</page>

<page sig="cc1v">
<wordgroup1 type="a" object="F"><alpha>F.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="fennel(n)">F<f type="i">Ennel</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is one of our <f type="i">Sallets</f> Furnitures which grows only from Seeds, and is seldom
transplanted. It resists the <f type="i">Cold</f> of <f type="i">Winter.</f> We sow it either in <f type="i">Beds</f> or Borders.
It springs again, when cut. Its youngest and tendrest shoots are the best. Its Seed is
gathered in <f type="i">August;</f> and in fine it agrees well enough with all sorts of Grounds. <f type="i">See
more of it under</f> Anis.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="furniture(n)"><f type="i">Furnitures,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">which are <f type="i">Mint, Tarragon, Samphire,</f> &amp;c. See their culture under the several
titles of those Plants that compose them.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="G"><alpha>G.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="garlic(n)">G<f type="i">Arlick</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="clove(n)">is propagated by heads, or kernels called <f type="i">Cloves,</f> about the end of <f type="i">February</f>
which are set three or four Inches deep in the Ground, and at three or four Inches
distance one from the other. They are taken out of the Earth at the end of <f type="i">July,</f> and
laid to dry in a place free from moisture, in order to preserve them from one year to
another.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="gooseberry(n)"><f type="i">Goose-berries:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Currans.</f></xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="H"><alpha>H.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="hartshorn(n)" lexeme="buck's horn(n)">H<f type="i">Artshorn</f> or <f type="i">Buckshorn Sallet,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a little annual Plant whose Leaves when tender, are
used in <f type="i">Sallet</f> Furnitures they are sown in <f type="i">March</f> very thick, it being impossible to
sow them thin, because their Seeds are so very small which are <emend err="gatherad">gathered</emend> in the Month
of <f type="i">August.</f> The little Birds are very greedy of them, as they are of all other small <f type="i">Kitchen</f>
Plant Seeds. When the Leaves of this Plant are cut, there spring up fresh ones, as do al&shy;
so from <f type="i">Sorrel, Cives, Parsley,</f> &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="fine herb(n)">Fine, or Sweet <f type="i">Herbs:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Aromaticks.</f></xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="L"><alpha>L.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="lavender(n)">L<f type="i">Avender</f></form> <xpln lang="en">serves to garnish borders in <f type="i">Kitchen-Gardens,</f> and yields a <f type="i">Flower</f> which with&shy;
out being separated from its stalks, is used to put among clean Linen to perfume
it. It is multiplied both by Seed, and by its Branches or Slips which have taken Root at
their joints.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="leek(n)"><f type="i">Leeks</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are sown at the end of <f type="i">Winter,</f> and that pretty thick, and in <f type="i">Beds</f> well prepared,
after which during the whole Month of <f type="i">June,</f> we take them up neatly and transplant
them into other <f type="i">Beds</f> which are no less carefully prepared; in order to which, we make
with a planting stick, holes about four Inches deep, and half a foot asunder, and after
we have a little trimmed both their Roots and Leaves, we only slide down a single
Plant into every hole, without minding to press down the Earth about it, as we do to
all other Plants; however we take care to grub up the Weeds about them from time
to time, and to water them a little in very dry weather, that their stems may grow to a
due thickness, and may whiten before <f type="i">Winter;</f> when the <f type="i">Frost</f> is very brisk, it is best to
cover them, or else, to set them into Earth in the <f type="i">Conservatory;</f> it is likewise very conve&shy;
nient, to take them up out of their <f type="i">Bed</f> where they are planted a little at large, and to
place them nearer together afterwards in another <f type="i">Nursery Bed,</f> and cover them up with
long Litter, because otherwise when it freezes hard, we should not be able to get them
out of the Ground without breaking them. We may leave some of them standing after
<f type="i">Winter,</f> to run to Seed, or else we may Plant some in a separate place for that purpose.
Their Seed is gathered in <f type="i">August,</f> and there is one sort that is bigger than the ordinary one,
which is the best.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="lettuce(n)"><f type="i">Lettuces</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="passion-lettuce(n)" lexeme="Roman lettuce(n)" lexeme="shell-lettuce(n)" lexeme="winter lettuce(n)" lexeme="curled bright lettuce(n)" lexeme="green curled lettuce(n)" lexeme="little red lettuce(n)" lexeme="short lettuce(n)" lexeme="royal lettuce(n)" lexeme="bellegarde lettuce(n)" lexeme="lettuce of Genoa(n)" lexeme="lettuce of Pergignan(n)" lexeme="lettuce of Auberviliers(n)" lexeme="imperial lettuce(n)" lexeme="green lettuce(n)" lexeme="red lettuce(n)" lexeme="alfange lettuce(n)" lexeme="George lettuce(n)" lexeme="Minion lettuce(n)" lexeme="Austrian lettuce(n)" lexeme="fair-looked lettuce(n)" lexeme="Autrichette(n)">are Plants that are the most ordinarily and commonly seen in our <f type="i">Kitchen-&shy;
Gardens,</f> and are indeed the most useful <f type="i">Manna</f> of them, and especially for <f type="i">Sallets,</f> of
which almost all the World is most constantly amorous. We have many things; for in
the first place, there are <f type="i">Lettuces</f> of different Seasons, those which are good in certain Months
in the year being not good in others; and those which grow well in the <f type="i">Spring,</f> thriving
not well in <f type="i">Summer;</f> and they which prosper in <f type="i">Autumn</f> and <f type="i">Winter,</f> coming <emend err="tonothing">to nothing</emend>, nei&shy;
ther in <f type="i">Spring</f> nor <f type="i">Summer,</f> as will be seen afterwards: In the second place, there are some
that with the ordinary help of the general culture attain their due perfection, and contri&shy;
bute both to the nourishment and pleasure of Man-kind, and they are the <f type="i">Cabbage Lettuces.</f>
</page>

<page sig="cc2r">
Thirdly, there are some that necessarily require the Art of Industry and the Gard'ner to
advance them to that degree of perfection which they should have; and they are such
as must be tied up to make them grow white, without which they would be neither
tender, nor sweet, nor good; such as are the <f type="i">Roman Lettuces,</f> &amp;c. Nay, and I have
thought fit sometimes to tie up those that were to cabbage, when I saw they did not cab&shy;
bage soon enough, by which means they may be forced to cabbage whether they will
or no. I use this Method particularly with some sorts of <f type="i">Winter Lettuces,</f> that is, when
there are any of them which though furnished with leaves big enough to cabbage,
yet for want of sufficient heat, are hindred from turning, that is from growing hard;
and this expedient is a very soveraign Remedy against that Defect, in a surly Season; and
besides these general distinctions, the number of the particular Kinds of <f type="i">Lettuces</f> is
greater than of any other sort of <f type="i">Kitchen-plants</f> whatsoever, as will appear more especially
by the order they observe in respect of the Seasons; and the order of the <f type="i">Cabbage-Lettuces,</f>
as near as I can describe it, is this.

The first that cabbage at the going out of Winter, are the <f type="i">Shell Lettuces</f> so call'd,
because their Leaf is round almost like a Shell. They are otherwise called <f type="i">Winter-Lettu&shy;
ces,</f> because they pretty well indure ordinary Frosts, which none of all the other <f type="i">Lettu&shy;
ces</f> can do. These are sown in <f type="i">September,</f> and afterward transplanted in some <f type="i">Wall-Border</f>
towards the South and East, in the Months of <f type="i">October</f> and <f type="i">November,</f> or else they are
sown upon <f type="i">Hot Beds</f> under <f type="i">Bells,</f> in the Months of <f type="i">February</f> and <f type="i">March,</f> and are good to
eat in <f type="i">April</f> and <f type="i">May.</f> We have at the same time another sort of <f type="i">Reddish Lettuces</f> called
<f type="i">Passion Lettuces,</f> which prosper very well in light Grounds, but not over well in others
that being colder and stronger or heavier, easily infect them with slimy Snivel. Both
these kinds should when they thrive, produce very thick and good Heads. To these
succeed the Bright curled <f type="i">Lettuces,</f> which usually cabbage in the Spring, that is before
the heat grows any thing excessive, but they must not be planted in strong and heavy Lands.
They likewise do well enough upon a <f type="i">Hot Bed,</f> and especially under <f type="i">Glass Bells</f> or <f type="i">Frames;</f>
for when they are sown in <f type="i">January,</f> and transplanted as soon as they are grown any thing
thick, or else left thin upon their <f type="i">Nursery-Beds,</f> they cabbage as soon as the <f type="i">Winter-Let&shy;
tuces,</f> and are very excellent.

There is about the same Season, two other sorts of <f type="i">Curled Bright Lettuces;</f> viz. one called
<f type="i">George Lettuces,</f> which are thicker and less curled than the ordinary <f type="i">Curled Bright Lettuces;</f>
and another called the <f type="i">Minion Lettuce,</f> which is the least sort. Both these last require such
Ground as we term good black Sand, but yet their Heads seldom cabbage close enough,
that is to say, are not ordinarily so hard and firm as those of the right <f type="i">Curled Bright Let&shy;
tuces.</f>

The <f type="i">Curled Green Lettuces</f> come in near about the same Season with the preceeding ones,
but are not so tender nor delicate.

There is also a sort of small red ones, and another named <f type="i">Short Lettuces,</f> both which
have all the necessary qualifications of good <f type="i">Lettuces,</f> excepting only that their
Heads are small, and that they likewise require Black Sandy Ground.

The first <f type="i">Lettuces</f> supply us amply as I have said, during the Months of <f type="i">April</f> and
<f type="i">May,</f> and the beginning of <f type="i">June,</f> but after that time they are too apt to be influenced
to run to Seed by the great heat that then comes on. They are followed during the
rest of <f type="i">June,</f> and all the Month of <f type="i">July,</f> by those called the <f type="i">Royal Bellegardes,</f> or <f type="i">Fair
Looks, Bright Genua's, Capucins, Aubervilliers,</f> and <f type="i">Perpignans;</f> of which last, there are both
green and bright, both of which produce very fair and very good heads, and thrive well
enough in strong Grounds too, when the Summer proves not too Rainy; but cold, or
too frequent Rains infect them with Slime and Snivel, and consequently destroy them. The
<f type="i">Capucin Lettuces</f> are reddish, cabbage easily, even without transplanting, and are pretty
delicate. The <f type="i">Aubervilliers</f> bring forth Heads that are too hard, and sometimes bitter
withall, and are more used for boiling than for Sallets. The difference that appears
between the <f type="i">Royal</f> and the <f type="i">Bellegardes</f> or <f type="i">Fair look'd Lettuces,</f> is only, that the former are a
little more Greenish, and these last a little Brighter.

However in the Summer time, the tied <f type="i">Lettuces</f> are mixed among the cabbaged ones,
<f type="i">viz.</f> the <f type="i">Roman Lettuces</f> which are open, and are called <f type="i">Chicons,</f> or <f type="i">Bright,</f> and are termed <f type="i">Al&shy;
phanges,</f> which last are more delicate than the <f type="i">Chicons,</f> both to raise, and when they are
eaten in <f type="i">Sallets.</f> There are also a sort which are stiled <f type="i">Imperial Lettuces,</f> which are of an ex&shy;
traordinary great Size, and are likewise very delicate to the taste, but very apt to rot
as soon as ever they grow white; there are besides, a certain kind of large <f type="i">Reddish Chi&shy;
cons,</f> which whiten in a manner of themselves without tying, and are good in course
Grounds, and succeed usually pretty well in Summer, for as for the green <f type="i">Chicons,</f> we
</page>

<page sig="cc2v">
cannot well have them but in the Spring, because they run too hastily to Seed. The
<f type="i">Lettuces</f> that defend themselves best from the great heat that predominates about the end
of <f type="i">July,</f> and all the Month of <f type="i">August,</f> are those we call <f type="i">Genua Lettuces,</f> and especially
the green sort, for the <f type="i">Bright Genua,</f> and <f type="i">Red Genua</f> run more easily to Seed, and will
hardly come to good but in light Grounds. We should therefore prepare a great many
of these green <f type="i">Genua's</f> against the Dog-days, and the first Frosts; we may also intermix
with them some few <f type="i">Bright,</f> and some <f type="i">Red Genua's;</f> but more especially we should
be sure to mix with them some <f type="i">Alfanges,</f> and a great quantity of bright or white <f type="i">Endive,</f>
as likewise, a great many <f type="i">Perpignan Lettuces,</f> both of the bright and green kind. The
great Inconveniencies that happen to <f type="i">Cabbage Lettuces,</f> are first, that they often degene&shy;
rate so far as to cabbage no more, which is discovered by their Leaves growing out in
length like a Cat's Tongue, as Gard'ners term it, or by their changing their natural
colour into another more or less green; and therefore we must be very careful to gather
no Seed from any but such as cabbage very well, for which effect, we should be sure
to mark out at first, some of those that turn best, in order to reserve them to run to
Seed where they stand, or to remove them with a turf of the Earth about them into some
separate place assigned for that purpose.

The second is, that as soon as the most part of them are cabbaged, they must be
spent, unless we would have the displeasure to see them run to Seed without doing us a&shy;
ny Service; in which Respect the Market Gard'ners have a great advantage beyond o&shy;
ther Persons, because they can sell off in one day, whole Beds of these <f type="i">Cabbage Lettuces,</f>
for commonly the Beds which were new planted at the same time, <f type="i">Cabbage</f> likewise all
at once, whereas in other Gardens, we cannot spend them any faster than we need them,
for which Reason we are obliged to plant often of them, and that in greater quantity
than we are able to consume, that we may have a continual supply of them successively,
without any Discontinuation, it being much more commodious to have an over-plus
quantity of them, than to want. The surest way is to keep particularly to those sorts
that are the most Rustical, and that last a great while cabbaged before they run to
Seed, such as are the <f type="i">Shell Lettuces,</f> the <f type="i">Perpignans,</f> the <f type="i">Green Genua's,</f> the <f type="i">Aubervilliers,</f>
and the <f type="i">Austrichettes,</f> or <f type="i">Austrian Lettuces,</f> which I must confess too, are a long time cab&shy;
baging.

The third inconvenience is, that the <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Morie</term>,</f> that is, the Rot which begins at
the ends of their Leaves, seizes them sometimes, and that when the Ground or the Sea&shy;
son are not favourable to them, they remain thin and lean, and run up to Seed instead
of spreading and cabbaging. There is hardly any Remedy to prevent this Rot be&shy;
cause there is hardly any to be found effectual against the cold and rainy Seasons that
cause it; but against the defects that may be in the Ground, there are infallible ones, that
is to say, it must be amended and improved with small Dung, if it be barren, whether it
be a sandy, or a <f type="i">Cold</f> and gross Earth; and to this last, we should give a little slope,
if when the Ground is good, the waters spoil it by settling too much in it, and by that
means, make all the Plants growing there to rot. Good Dung throughly rotten, be&shy;
ing the Soul and <f type="i"><term lang="la">Primum Mobile</term></f> of <f type="i">Kitchen-Gardens,</f> without which, no more than
without frequent waterings, and dressing of the Ground no man can ever be rich in fine
and goodly <f type="i">Legumes.</f>

There yet remains to be known for the perfect understanding the ordering of <f type="i">Let&shy;
tuces,</f> that they which grow biggest must be placed ten or twelve inches one from the
other, which is to be understood of the <f type="i">Shell Lettuces, Perpignans, Austrians, Bellegardes,</f>
or <f type="i">Fair-looks, Aubervilliers, Alfanges,</f> and <f type="i">Imperials;</f> and for those that bear heads but of a
midling size, the distance of seven or eight inches is enough, which are the Bright Curled,
the short, the little <f type="i">Red,</f> and the <f type="i">Green Chicon Lettuces,</f> &amp;c. Those that will be good
husbands may sow <f type="i">Radishes</f> in their <f type="i">Lettuce Beds,</f> because the <f type="i">Radishes</f> will be all drawn
out and spent before the <f type="i">Lettuces</f> cabbage; and for the same reason, because the <f type="i">Endives</f>
are much longer before they come to perfection than the <f type="i">Lettuces,</f> we may Plant some of
these last among the <f type="i">Endives;</f> they agree well enough one with the other: and so we
may have a double crop to gather upon one and the same <f type="i">Bed</f> and in the same Season;
for the <f type="i">Lettuces</f> are gathered first, and afterwards the <f type="i">Endives</f> arrive to their full good&shy;
ness.</xpln><lemenote>"Austrian lettuce" and "Autrichettes" not found in OED. "George lettuce" antedates earliest OED citation (1706).</lemenote></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</page>

<page sig="cc3r">
<wordgroup1 type="a" object="M"><alpha>M.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h" lexeme="m&acirc;che(n)"><form lang="en">M<f type="i">Aches,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are a sort of little <f type="i">Sallet</f> which we may call a wild and rustical
<f type="i">Sallet,</f> because indeed it seldom is brought before any noble Company.
They are multiplied by Seed which is gathered in <f type="i">July,</f> and are only used towards the end
of <f type="i">Winter.</f> We make <f type="i">Beds</f> for them which we sow about the end of <f type="i">August;</f> they are
hardy enough to resist the rigours of the <f type="i">Frost,</f> and because they produce a great many
little Seeds that easily fall, though we have but a little quantity of them, they will propa&shy;
gate themselves sufficiently, without any other culture but weeding them.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="mallow(n)" lexeme="marshmellow(n)"><f type="i">Mallows</f> and <f type="i">Marsh-mallows</f></form> <xpln lang="en">ought to be allowed a place in our <f type="i">Kitchen-Gardens,</f> though
civility will not permit us to explain in this Treatise what uses they serve for, and though
they be rather Plants of the wild fields than of a <f type="i">Garden.</f> They grow of their own accord,
and have no more need of cultivating than any of the Weeds that infest the good Herbs.
When we have a mind to have any of them in our <f type="i">Gardens,</f> it will be best to sow them in
some by-place.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="marjoram(n)"><f type="i">Marjoram,</f> or <f type="i">Marjerom,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is an Odoriferous Plant of which we compose agreeable
Borders and Edgings. There is the <f type="i">Winter Marjoram,</f> which is the best, and the <f type="i">Summer
Marjoram</f> which lasts not beyond that Season. Both of them are multiplied by Seed,
and likewise by Rooted slips or suckers. They are principally used in making Per&shy;
fumes.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="balm(n)" lexeme="mint(n)"><f type="i">Mint,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">called in <f type="i">French, Balm,</f> when once planted, needs no other particular culture
than being cut down close to the Ground every year, at the end of <f type="i">Autumn,</f> to make it
shoot out store of tender Sprouts in the <f type="i">Spring,</f> which are mingled with the Furnitures
of <f type="i">Sallets</f> for them that love them a little spicy and perfumed. It must be renewed
every three years at least, and placed always in good Earth. The Branches when cut
off, take Root at the place where they are covered, and by that means, of one great
Tuft, we may easily make a great many, which are to be planted at the distance of a
foot one from the other. In the <f type="i">Winter</f> likewise, we plant some thick Tufts of it upon
<f type="i">Hot Beds</f> and by taking care to cover them with <f type="i">Bells,</f> they spring very well for about fif&shy;
teen days, and then perish.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="muscat(n)" lexeme="long muscat(n)" lexeme="chasselas(n)"><f type="i">Muscats</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are a kind of <f type="i">Grapes,</f> which when they attain to their natural goodness, are
one of the most considerable commodities of a <f type="i">Kitchen-Garden.</f> There are three sorts of
them, <f type="i">viz.</f> White, Red, and Black, the White is commonly the best of the three, it re&shy;
quires temperate Countries like that of the <f type="i">Isle</f> of <f type="i">France,</f> and the Expositions of the
<f type="i">South</f> and <f type="i">East,</f> and always a light Ground, we seldom see any good ones in pure Earth,
and if it be in hot Climates, or Gravelly, and Sandy Ground; they prosper very well upon
Counter-Espaliers, or Pole Hedge-Trees, and even in the open Air. Their Goodness
consists in having large, yellow, and crackling Berries, and growing thin in their Clu&shy;
sters, and in a pretty rich musked taste, but yet not too strong like the <f type="i">Spanish</f> ones.
The Province of <f type="i">Tourain</f> produces admirable ones. Their Culture is exactly the same
with that of the <f type="i">Chassela's</f> Grapes, both as to their Pruning, and manner of Propaga&shy;
tion.

The <f type="i">Long Muscat,</f> called otherwise the <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Passe-Musquee</term>,</f> is another sort of Grape whose
Berry is bigger and more longish than that of the ordinary <f type="i">Muscat,</f> and its clusters are
also longer, but yet its taste is nothing near so rich as that of the others.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="N"><alpha>N.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="nasturces(n)">N<f type="i">Asturces:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Capucin Capers.</f></xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="O"><alpha>O.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="onion(n)">O<f type="i">Nions</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are red or white, which last are sweeter and more prized than the red ones.
There's no Body but knows how many uses they serve for. They are propagated
only by Seed, which is commonly sown at the latter end of <f type="i">February</f> and beginning of
<f type="i">March,</f> in Beds of good Earth, and well prepared, and afterwards raked with an Iron
Rake, to cover them, as is done to other small Seeds. They must be sown thin, that they
may have room to grow to their full bigness, and therefore if they come up too thick, they
must be thinned by pulling some of them up as soon as they are big enough for that,
which is towards the Month of <f type="i">May,</f> which we transplant in order to use instead of <f type="i">Ci&shy;
bouls.</f> Though the ordinary Season for sowing <f type="i">Onions,</f> be at the end of Winter, yet we
</page>

<page sig="cc3v">
may sow some in <f type="i">September,</f> and transplant them afterwards in the Month of <f type="i">May,</f> by which
means we may have some full grown at the very beginning of <f type="i">July,</f> which we may ga&shy;
ther, plucking them first out of the Ground as soon as that time comes, and then after
we have dried them two or three days in the hot Sun, lay them up in some dry place, to
keep all the year in case of need. We must not forget when our <f type="i">Onions</f> begin to appear
with pretty thick Stems above the Superficies of the Earth, that is, when they begin to
advance towards their Maturity, to break them down, either by treading them under our
Feet, or with a board pretty hard pressed down upon them, because by that means the
nourishment that was before spent in their stems, being hindered from mounting upwards,
will remain and settle all in that which I think, is but improperly called their head, and
make it grow so much the bigger. I have already told you elsewhere, how their Seed
is to be raised.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="P"><alpha>P.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="parsley(n)">P<f type="i">Arsley,</f> as well of the Curled as ordinary sort,</form> <xpln lang="en">is of great use in Kitchens all the year
long, as well for its Leaves as Roots. It is comprehended under the Title of <f type="i">Ver&shy;
dures</f> or green <f type="i">Pot-herbs.</f> We ought not to fail in the Spring, to sow a reasonable quanti&shy;
ty of it in every Garden, and that pretty thick, and in good, and well prepared Ground.
When its Leaves are cut, it shoots out new ones like Sorrel. It well enough resists a mo&shy;
derate, but not a violent cold, and therefore 'tis best to bestow some covering on it in
Winter, to defend it. When we would have any of it produce large Roots, we must thin
it in the Beds or Borders where 'tis sown. It requires pretty much watering in very hot
weather. There are some that pretend to have a kind of <f type="i">Parsly</f> bigger than ordinary,
but for my part I know no such kind. The Curled <f type="i">Parsly</f> appears more agreeable to the
Sight, than the Common sort, but is never a whit better than it for that. We gather
our <f type="i">Parsly</f> Seeds in the Months of <f type="i">August</f> and <f type="i">September.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Macedonian parsley(n)" lexeme="alexanders(n)"><f type="i">Macedonian Parsley,</f> or <f type="i">Alisanders,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is one of the Furnitures of our <f type="i">Winter-Sallets,</f> which
must be whitened like <f type="i">Wild Endive,</f> or <f type="i">Succory;</f> that is to say, at the end of <f type="i">Autumn,</f> we
must cut down all its Leaves, and then cover the Bed where it grows, all over with
long dry Dung, or Straw Screens, so close, that the Frost may not come at it, by which
means, the new Leaves that spring from it, grow white, yellowish and tender. We
sow it in the Spring, pretty thin, because it produces a great many large Leaves, and
we gather its Seed at the latter end of Summer. It is a good hardy Plant, and that de&shy;
fends its self very well from the Drought, without requiring much watering.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="parsnip(n)"><f type="i">Parsnips</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are a sort of Roots well known in our <f type="i">Kitchens.</f> We sow them towards the
end of <f type="i">Winter,</f> either in open Ground, or Borders, and that always pretty thin, and in
good and well prepared Ground; and if they come up too thick, they must be thinned
as soon as <f type="i">May</f> comes in, that they which are left, may be the better nourisht, and
grow the fairer. They are propagated only by Seed, for the raising which, the same
care is to be taken as we have directed for that of <f type="i">Red Beet-roots, Carrots,</f> &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr" lexeme="passe-musque(n)"><f type="i">Passe Musquee:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Muscats.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="patience(n)" lexeme="sharp dock(n)"><f type="i">Patience,</f> or <f type="i">Sharp Dock:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Dock.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="pea(n)"><f type="i">Peas,</f> or <f type="i">Pease</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="hastings(n)">may be placed in rank of <f type="i">Kitchen-plants.</f> It is a good rustical or hardy
Plant, which commonly is sown in the open Field, without needing any other Culture
than being weeded whilst 'tis young, that is, before it begins to codd. But when
they are propt, they yield more than when they are not. They require pretty
good Ground, and a little Rain to make them tender and delicate, and must be sown
pretty thin. There are several sorts of them, <f type="i">viz. Hastings, Green, White</f> and <f type="i">Square
ones,</f> otherwise called large codded <f type="i">Peas,</f> &amp;c. We may have of them in the Months of
<f type="i">May, June, July, August, September</f> and <f type="i">October.</f> For to have some all that while after
the first, we have no more to do, but to sow them in different Months, to have them
fit for eating three Months after. Those sorts we are most choice of in <f type="i">Kitchen-Gardens</f>
are the <f type="i">Hastings</f> both White and Green, which are of a midling Size. We sow them at
the end of <f type="i">October,</f> under the shelter of some <f type="i">Eastern</f> or <f type="i">Southern</f> Walls, and we raise
Ridges or sloped Banks too, sometimes for that purpose; and to dispose them to come up
so much the sooner when they are Sown, we make them Sprout five or six days before,
by laying them to steep two days in Water, and afterwards laying them in a place
where the cold cannot reach them, till their first Root begins to appear. Hard wea&shy;
ther spoils them quite, which is the reason why all we can do, will not procure us any
good ones till the latter end of <f type="i">May.</f> We likewise sow some upon hot Beds, at the end
of <f type="i">February,</f> in order to transplant them by the sides of some well exposed Walls, in
</page>

<page sig="cc4r">
case those sown at the latter end of <f type="i">October</f> preceeding, happen to have been spoiled by
the Frost. Our last time of Sowing them is at <f type="i">Midsummer,</f> to have them fit to eat a&shy;
bout <f type="i">All-hallow-tide.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="pompion(n)" lexeme="potiron(n)"><f type="i">Pompions</f> and <f type="i">Potirons,</f> or <f type="i">Flat Pompions:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Citrulls.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="purslane(n)"><f type="i">Purslain</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is one of the prettiest Plants in a <f type="i">Kitchen-gardens,</f> which is principally used in
<f type="i">Sallets,</f> and sometimes in <f type="i">Pottages.</f> There are two sorts of it, <f type="i">viz.</f> The <f type="i">Green,</f> and the
<f type="i">Red,</f> or <f type="i">Golden;</f> this latter is the more agreeable of the two to the Eye, and more deli&shy;
cate and difficult to rear, so that in hard weather we have much ado to make it grow
even upon hot Beds, and under Bells, for it seldom prospers in open Beds till about the
middle of <f type="i">May,</f> and then too, the Earth must be very good, sweet, and very loose,
and the weather very fair. And therefore for our first <f type="i">Purslain</f> which we are not to
begin to sow upon hot Beds till towards the Middle of <f type="i">March,</f> we must use only the
Green sort, because the Yellow or Golden sort dwindles away as soon as 'tis come up, un&shy;
less the Season be a little advanced, and the Sun a little hot, which is, towards the end
of <f type="i">April.</f> It is commonly sown very thick, because its Seed is so very small, that it can&shy;
not be sown thin. When we sow it upon hot Beds, either when 'tis cold, and that
by consequence Glass-bells or Frames are needfull, or in milder Weather, we only press
down the Mold about it with our hands, or with the back of a Spade; but when we
sow it in open Beds which must be well prepared for that purpose, we rake it over
five or six times with an Iron Rake, to make the Seed enter into the Ground.

<emend err="They">The</emend> way to raise Seed from it, is to transplant some Plants of it that are big e&shy;
nough, into Beds well prepared, at the distance of eight or ten inches ones from the o&shy;
ther: The Months of <f type="i">June</f> and <f type="i">July</f> are proper for that effect. And then in a little
time after, they are run up, and have done flowring, assoon as ever we perceive any
of their Husks to open, and discover some black Seed, we must cut down all their
Stems and lay them some days in the Sun till all the Seed be quite ripened, and then we
beat them out and winnow them, <f type="i">&amp;c.</f> We must be carefull to transplant each sort a&shy;
part by it self, that we may not be mistaken in the Seed when we are to sow it. The
Stick Stalks of <f type="i">Purslain</f> that is run to Seed, are good to pickle in Salt and Vinegar, for
Winter Sallets.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="R"><alpha>R</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="radish(n)">R<f type="i">Adishes,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">when they are qualified with all the goodness they should have, that is,
when they are tender, and snap easily, and are sweet, are in my Opinion, one of
the Plants that give the most pleasure of any in our <f type="i">Kitchin-gardens,</f> and that give it as
often, and for as long a time as any of them all; and I look upon them as a kind of
<f type="i">Manna</f> in our Gardens. There seems to be no great pains required to make them grow,
it being indeed only necessary to sow them pretty thin, in well prepared loose and mel&shy;
low Earth, and to water them soundly in drie Weather, and with this culture they
will attain to all the perfection they are capable of. But the main points here in Que&shy;
stion, are first, to be always provided with Seed of a good kind; and secondly, to take
order to have <f type="i">Radishes</f> without discontinuation, from the Month of <f type="i">February,</f> till the
coming in of the Frosts in the middle of <f type="i">November.</f> As for Seed of a good kind, know,
that is it that produces few Leaves, and a long red Root, for there are some that pro&shy;
duce a great many leaves and little Root; and when we are once provided with Seed
of a good kind, we must be extreme carefull to propagate it, that we never be without
a stock of it; for which effect, in the Month of <f type="i">April,</f> we must choose out among
those <f type="i">Radishes</f> that are come of the last years Seed, such, as I have said, which have the
fewest Leaves and the most Root, and reddest Necks, and transplant them quite whole
in some well prepared spot of Ground, a foot and a half a sunder: Being so transplanted,
they will run up, flower and yield Seed ripe enough to gather towards the end of <f type="i">July;</f>
and then we cut down their stems; and after they have been dried some days in the
Sun, we beat out the Seed, and winnow it, <f type="i">&amp;c.</f>

Those stocks of them that run up to Seed, shoot up their Branches to such a height,
and perpetuate their flowers so far as if they knew not where to stop; and therefore it is
good to pinch off these Branches to a reasonable length, that the first Podds may be the
better nourished.

But 'tis not enough to raise good Seed, we must likewise take order to be supplied
with good <f type="i">Radishes</f> for eight or nine Months in the year. The first that are eaten grow
on Hot-beds, the manner of raising which, I have explained in the Works of <f type="i">November;</f>
and by the means of those <f type="i">Hot Beds,</f> we may have some during the Months of <f type="i">Februa&shy;
</page>

<page sig="cc4v">
ry</f> <f type="i">March</f> and <f type="i">April;</f> otherwise we have none; and in order to have some all the other
Months, we must sow some among all manner of Seeds, they coming up so very quickly,
that we have time to gather off our <f type="i">Radishes</f> before they can do any harm to the other
Plants. <f type="i">Radishes</f> are extreamly apprehensive of the excessive heat in <f type="i">Summer,</f> which makes
them grow strong as they term it, that is, too biting, stringy, and sometimes very hard;
and therefore in that Season we would affect to sow them in very loose Mellow Ground,
where the Sun shines but little; and the best way should be, to make up along by the
sides of some <f type="i">Northern</f> Walls, a <f type="i">Bed</f> or two for that purpose, filled with mold to the depth
of a large foot and an half, and to sow our <f type="i">Radishes</f> there, and water them well. In
<f type="i">Spring</f> and <f type="i">Autumn,</f> when the Sun is not so <f type="i">Hot, Radishes</f> take well enough in open Ground,
and in the wide unsheltered Air.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="raspberry(n)"><f type="i">Rass-berries,</f> or <f type="i">Rasp-berries,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">as well as the <f type="i">White</f> as <f type="i">Red,</f> begin to Ripen at the begin&shy;
ning of <f type="i">July.</f> They are planted in <f type="i">March,</f> either in <f type="i">Beds</f> or borders observing the
distance of two foot between Plant and Plant. They shoot out during the <f type="i">Summer</f>
many well Rooted Suckers, some of which we take away to make new plantations with,
by which means the old ones are likewise renewed for they drie assoon as their
<f type="i">Fruit</f> is gathered. The only culture used to them is, first, in the Month of <f type="i">March</f>
to shorten all their new shoots which we preserve round about the old stock, and which
ought to be only the thickest and handsomest, and in the second place, to pluck away all
the small ones, as likewise the old ones that are dead.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="reponce(n)"><f type="i">Reponces</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="radish(n)">are a sort of small sweet <f type="i">Radishes</f> which grow wild in the Country, and
especially in the <f type="i">Corn,</f> and are eaten in <f type="i">Sallets</f> in the spring time. They are multiplied only
by Seed.</xpln><lemenote>"responces" was not found in OED.</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="rocambole(n)"><f type="i">Rocamboles:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Shallots.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="rocket(n)"><f type="i">Rocket</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is one of our <f type="i">Sallet</f> Furnitures, which is sown in the <f type="i">Spring</f> as most of the others
are. Its Leaf is pretty like that of <f type="i">Radishes,</f> and its Seed is very small, and almost like
<f type="i">Purslain</f> Seed, but it is of a <f type="i">Reddish,</f> or rather darkish <f type="i">Cinnamon Colour.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="rosemary(n)"><f type="i">Rosemary</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is another sort of Odoriferous Plant which is principally used for the per&shy;
fuming of <f type="i">Chambers,</f> and in decoctions for washing the Feet. It is multiplied in
the same manner as <f type="i">Rue,</f> and other border Plants, and lasts five or six years in its
place.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="rue(n)"><f type="i">Rue</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a Plant of very strong smell, of which we plant some borders in our <f type="i">Gardens;</f>
it is propagated both by Seed, and Rooted slips, and is hardly of any use but against the
vapours of the Mother.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="S"><alpha>S.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="sage(n)">S<f type="i">Age</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is a border Plant, whose culture has nothing of particular, but is like that of the
other border Herbs, as <f type="i">Rosemary, Lavender, Worm-wood,</f> &amp;c. There is a sort that is parti-&shy;
Coloured, which to some people appears more agreeable than the common <f type="i">Sage,</f> which is
of palish <f type="i">Green</f> Colour.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Spanish salsify(n)"><f type="i">Spanish Salsifie,</f> or <f type="i">Sassifie,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="scorzonera(n)">otherwise <f type="i">Scorzonere,</f> is one of our chiefest Roots, which is
multiplied by Seed as well as the others, and is admirable good boiled both for the plea&shy;
sure of the taste, and the health of the Body. It is propagated only by Seed which is
sown in <f type="i">March.</f> We must be careful to sow it pretty thin, whether it be in <f type="i">Beds</f> or
borders, or else at least to thin it afterward, that its Roots may grow the bigger.
<f type="i">Scorzonere</f> runs up to Seed in the Months of <f type="i">June</f> and <f type="i">July,</f> and is gathered assoon as 'tis
Ripe.</xpln><lemenote>Antedates earliest OED citation for "Spanish salsify" (1707).</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="salsify(n)">Common <f type="i">Salsifie</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is another sort of Root cultivated after the same manner as the preceed&shy;
ing one, but is not altogether so very excellent. They easily pass the <f type="i">Winter</f> in the
Ground. It is good to water both sorts of them in very dry weather, and to keep them
well weeded, and especially, to put them into good Earth well prepared, of at least two
full foot deep.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="samphire(n)"><f type="i">Samphire</f></form> <xpln lang="en">called in <f type="i">French, <term lang="fr">Pierce Pierre</term>,</f> or <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Passe-Pierre</term>,</f> is one of our <f type="i">Sallet</f> Furnitures
that is multiplied only by Seed, and which being by nature very delicate requires to be
planted by the sides of Walls exposed to the <f type="i">South</f> or <f type="i">East,</f> the open Air, and great <f type="i">Cold</f>
being pernicious to it. We usually sow it in some Pot or Tub filled with mold, or
else on some side-Bank towards the <f type="i">South</f> or <f type="i">East,</f> and that in the Months of <f type="i">March</f> or
<f type="i">April,</f> and afterwards transplant it in those places above-mentioned.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="savory(n)"><f type="i">Savory</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is an annual Plant a little Odoriferous, which grows only from Seed, and whose
Leaves are used to some <f type="i">Ragou's,</f> and particularly among <f type="i">Peas, Beans;</f> it is sown in the
<f type="i">Spring</f> either in <f type="i">Beds</f> or borders.</xpln></wordentry>
</page>

<page sig="dd1r">
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="scorzonera(n)"><f type="i">Scorzonere,</f> or <f type="i">Scorzonera:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Spanish Salsifie.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="shalot(n)"><f type="i">Shallots,</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="rocambole(n)" lexeme="Spanish garlic(n)">otherwise <f type="i">Rocamboles,</f> or <f type="i">Spanish Garlick,</f> require no other culture than com&shy;
mon <f type="i">Garlick,</f> and are particularly remarkable for that their Seeds are as good to eat
as their Cloves taken out of the Earth. Their Seed is large and serves to propagate them
as well as the Cloves or Kernels that compose their Root.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="skirret(n)"><f type="i">Skirrets</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are a sort of Roots propagated by Seed, and cultivated like other Roots, as is
directed in the Month of <f type="i">March.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="spinach(n)"><f type="i">Spinage</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is one of those <f type="i">Kitchen</f> Plants that requires the best Ground, or at least that
which is most amended and improved. They are multiplied only by Seed. We sow them
either in open Ground, or else in furrows or strait rows upon well prepared <f type="i">Beds</f> and
this we do several times in the year, beginning about the sixteenth of <f type="i">August,</f> and finish&shy;
ing a Month after; the first are fit to cut towards the middle of <f type="i">October,</f> the second in
<f type="i">Lent,</f> and the last in <f type="i">Rogation</f> time; Those which remain after <f type="i">Winter,</f> run up to Seed
towards the end of <f type="i">May,</f> which we gather about the middle of <f type="i">June.</f> When they are
once cut they spring up no more, as <f type="i">Sorrel</f> do's. All their culture consists in keeping
them very clear from Weeds; and if the <f type="i">Autumn</f> prove extraordinary dry, it is not amiss
to water them sometimes. They are never transplanted no more than <f type="i">Chervil, Cresses,</f>
&amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="sorrel(n)"><f type="i">Sorrel</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="verdure(n)" lexeme="green pot herb(n)">in <f type="i">Kitchen-Garden</f> terms, is placed under the title of <f type="i">Verdures,</f> or <f type="i">Green</f> Pot Herbs,
and accordingly is much used in the Pot. There are some sorts of it that produce a lar&shy;
ger Leaf than others, which are called <f type="i">Sorrel</f> of the greater sort. All the sorts may be
sown in the Months of <f type="i">March, April, May, June, July</f> and <f type="i">August,</f> and in the beginning
of <f type="i">September</f> too, provided they be allowed sufficient time to grow big enough to resist
the rigour of the <f type="i">Winter,</f> we sow <f type="i">Sorrel</f> either in open Ground, or else in strait rows, or
furrows, in <f type="i">Beds</f> or borders, in all which cases, it must be sown very thick, because
many of its Plants perish. It requires a ground that is naturally good, or else well im&shy;
proved with <f type="i">Muck.</f> Its culture consists in being kept very clear of Weeds, in being well
watered, and being covered with a little mold once or twice a year, after 'tis first cut
down very close to the Ground. That mold serves to give it new vigour, and the Sea&shy;
son most proper for applying it, is in the hot Months of the year. <f type="i">Sorrel</f> is most com&shy;
monly multiplied by Seed, though sometimes we transplant some of it that thrives very
well. We gather its Seed in the Months of <f type="i">July</f> and <f type="i">August.</f> There is a particular sort
of <f type="i">Sorrel,</f> which is called Round <f type="i">Sorrel,</f> its Leaves being indeed Round, whereas those of
the other sorts are very sharp and pointed. The tender Leaves of this sort are sometimes
mixed with <f type="i">Sallet</f> Furnitures. But it is ordinarily used most in <f type="i">Bouillons,</f> or thin Broths.
It is multiplied by running Branches, that take Root in the Earth, as they run over it,
which being taken off, and transplanted, produce thick Tufts which also produce other
runners, and so in <f type="i"><term lang="la">infinitum</term>.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="sharp dock(n)">Sharp <f type="i">Dock,</f> or <f type="i">Dock-Sorrel:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Dock.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="wood-sorrel(n)">Wood <f type="i">Sorrel,</f> or <f type="i">Alleluia:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Alleluia.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="strawberry(n)"><f type="i">Straw-berries,</f> as well the <f type="i">White</f> as the <f type="i">Red,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">multiply and perpetuate themselves by
running Suckers that springing out of their old stocks, take Root. It is observed, that a
new plantation of them taken out of the Woods, turns to better account when transplan&shy;
ted, than one slipt of from the <f type="i">Garden Straw-berries.</f> We plant them either in <f type="i">Beds</f> or
borders, both which must be well prepared, amended and laboured or stirred up in one
manner or other. If it be in dry and sandy Ground, both the <f type="i">Beds</f> and borders must
be sunk a little lower than the Allies or path-ways, the better to retain both the rain that
falls, and the water we bestow on them; a quite contrary course must be taken, if we
plant them in strong, heavy, and fat Earth, and that is almost all pure Clay, because
excessive moisture rots the Plants. We place them usually nine or ten Inches asunder,
putting two or three little Plants into each hole which we make with a planting stick.
The best time to plant them in is during the whole Month of <f type="i">May,</f> and in the beginning
of <f type="i">June,</f> that is to say, before the great heat comes in. And we may plant them all
<f type="i">Summer</f> long in rainy Seasons. It is particularly requisite to plant <f type="i">Nurseries</f> of them in the
Month of <f type="i">May,</f> and that in some place near the <f type="i">North</f> Quarter, the better to shelter them
from the violent heat of the <f type="i">Summer</f> Sun, and then we plant them but three or four Inches
one from the other, and when they are grown big enough there, we transplant them
afterwards in the Month of <f type="i">September</f> in order to make <f type="i">Beds</f> or squares of them, accor&shy;
ding as we find occasion to have a greater or less abundance of them. Their culture
consists chiefly, first, in watering them well in dry Seasons; secondly in leaving, but a
moderate number of stems or upright shoots to every stock, three or four of the most
vigourous being enough; in the third place, in leaving but three or four <f type="i">Straw-berries</f> of
</page>

<page sig="dd1v">
them that appeared first, and nearest the stock, on every stem, and therefore we
must pinch of all their other Blossoms that almost endlessly grow out at the ends of those
that have already Blossomed, or are still in Blossom, because none but the first produce any
fair <f type="i">Straw-berries,</f> hardly any of the last being ever known to knit or come to any per&shy;
fection, but when we are careful to pinch them off judicially we may be assured always
to have good <f type="i">Straw-berries.</f> I have already given directions in the works of the Month
of <f type="i">February,</f> how to raise <f type="i">Hasting Straw-berries.</f> Curious Persons have usually two
<f type="i">Straw-berries</f> of two several <f type="i">Colours,</f> viz. <f type="i">Red</f> and <f type="i">White,</f> but they place them in separate
<f type="i">Beds.</f> The great Enemies of <f type="i">Straw-berry</f> Plantations are the <f type="i">Ton's</f> which are great
<f type="i">White</f> Worms, that in the Months of <f type="i">May</f> and <f type="i">June,</f> gnaw the necks of their Roots
between two Earths, and so kill them; to prevent which, in those Months we should
carefully search every day, under the Roots of all our <f type="i">Straw-berries</f> that begin to wither,
where we shall commonly find one of these great Worms which after they have done a
mischief to one, pass on to do the same to other <f type="i">Straw-berry</f> Plants, and kill them in the
same manner. <f type="i">Straw-berry</f> Plants bear very well the year after their planting, if plan&shy;
ted in <f type="i">May;</f> but yield very indifferently, if not planted till <f type="i">September,</f> after they are taken
out of the Woods; yet in the second year they bear wonderfully, but that being past,
they produce but very pitifully, and therefore 'tis good to renew them every two years;
it is likewise convenient to cut off every year their old tops, when the <f type="i">Straw-berries</f> are
gone, which is commonly at the latter end of <f type="i">July.</f> The earliest <f type="i">Straw-berries</f> that ri&shy;
pen towards the end of <f type="i">May,</f> are those that were planted by the sides of <f type="i">Southern</f> or <f type="i">Ea&shy;
stern</f> Walls, and they that ripen last, are such as are planted in a <f type="i">Northern Exposition.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="succory(n)"><f type="i">Succory:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Endive.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="sweet herb(n)"><f type="i">Sweet Herbs:</form> <xpln lang="en">See Aromaticks.</f></xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="T"><alpha>T.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="tarragon(n)">T<f type="i">Arragon</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is one of the perfuming or Spicy Furnitures of our <f type="i">Sallets,</f> it is propagated
both by rooted slips and Seed. It springs again several times after 'tis cut; it endures
the Winter, and needs little watering in the driest weather in Summer, when we plant
it, we must allow eight or nine inches distance between Plant and Plant in the Beds we set
with it. The best time to plant in, is in <f type="i">March</f> and <f type="i">April,</f> which hinders not, but that
we may transplant it again in the Summer Season.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="thyme(n)"><f type="i">Time</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is another odoriferous Border-plant, which is multiplied as well by Seed as rooted
Branches or Slips. A Border of <f type="i">Time</f> is a considerable and necessary Ornament in our
<f type="i">Kitchen-Gardens.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="trip-madam(n)"><f type="i">Tripe-Madam</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is one of our <f type="i">Sallet-Furnitures;</f> it is used chiefly in the Spring when it is
tender, but a little of it ought to serve in the Summer, because then it is too tough. It
is multiplyed both by Seeds and Cuttings.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="turnip(n)"><f type="i">Turneps</f> or <f type="i">Turnips</f></form> <xpln lang="en">are not properly <f type="i">Kitchen-Garden</f> Plants, but yet where they are spa&shy;
cious, they may be admitted into them. They are propagated only by Seed, and are
sown very thick in Beds, some in <f type="i">March,</f> and others in <f type="i">August.</f> We gather their Seed
in <f type="i">July</f> and <f type="i">August,</f> every Body so well knows their use, that I need speak no more of
them here.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="V"><alpha>V.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="verjuice grape(n)">V<f type="i">erjuice Grapes:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See <f type="i">Bourdelais.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="vine(n)"><f type="i">Vines:</f></form> <xpln lang="en">See the several heads of <f type="i">Bourdelais, Muscats,</f> &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="violet(n)"><f type="i">Violets,</f></form> <xpln lang="en">and especially the double ones serve to make pretty Borders in our <f type="i">Kitchen-&shy;
Gardens.</f> Their flowers make a very agreeable Figure when they are artfully placed on
the Superficies of Spring-Sallets. Every Body knows, that they are propagated by
Tufts, that is, by dividing one great Tuft into several little ones, which likewise in
time grows thick, and fit to be divided into other little ones.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="W"><alpha>W.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="wormwood(n)">W<f type="i">Ormwood.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">The plants of this and all other Plants placed in Borders or Edgings,
and therefore called Border-plants, as of <f type="i">Time, Lavender, Hyssop,</f> &amp;c. are plan&shy;
ted by a Line, and at the distance of two or three Inches one from the other, and five
or six inches deep in the Ground. It is good to clip them every Spring, and to renew
them every two years, and to take away their oldest and decayed stocks. Their Seed
is gathered about the
Month of <f type="i">August.</f></xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</section>
</page>
</leme>
